O¶

O for awesome¶

phr. [Boxer David Tua's famous request on Wheel of Fortune 1992]

(Apr 9, 2011,Dominion Post,B1)

Asked to choose a vowel, he paused for a moment before asking for an "O for awesome". (Apr 9, 2011,Dominion Post,B1)

O'Briens¶

1 n. (Rural) distinctive farm boots, particularly for mustering [orig. trade name.]

LOUGHNAN O'Briens: see under Pannells and R and Gs. All are terms for boots. (1981,Loughnan Glossary,56)

"O'Briens and "Pannells" also have a place as distinctive boots worn by country folk, and particularly those mustering sheep. (Feb 11, 1977,Press, The,19)

SINCLAIR & TROTTER I was lucky I was well shod, O'Brien mustering boots on, my saddletweed trousers tied down, so I was dry as could be. (1994,From Caithness to Southland,17)

O'Leary¶

n.

SUTTON-SMITH There are games recorded in this period for which there is no record before 1900 though they may well have existed under other names. West Coast: Dunk and Davey, Spang Weazling; Wellington: Who Goes Round My Stone Wall, Beg o' My Neighbor, Bellahonie, Run a Mile, On and Off the Bridge, Here Come Two Nuns, Colours, Isaac and Rebecca. Scrag, Diabolo, Bumble Puppy, Homai, Fives; Auckland: My Fair Young Lady, Rushes and Reeds, Surrender the Tower, Walls, Ball Hopscotch, ball games, Solomon Says; Hawkes Bay: Wolf Wolf Go Home, Homai; Canterbury: Poor Old Tom, Rats and Rabbits and Rips, Steps, Skin the Snake, Steal a Peg, Toodle-em-Buck, The Gag; Golden Bay: Who's That Walking Round My House Tonight, I've Come to See Poor Mary Jane, Steps and Stairs, Creeping, Branding, O'Leary; Otago: Sir Roger de Coverley, Who's That Knocking at My Door. Ibid. 273 Ball-bouncing games have come into their own on the asphalt playgrounds of modern schools and are played in many schools throughout the country. There is no evidence of such games before 1900, but a number of instances between 1900 and 1920 are recorded. The games were known as Fives, Tens, Sevens, and Walls and have been recorded more often since under the names O'Leary or Sevens. Before 1920 there is only one report of the game being played with a rhyme, and this was a rhyme more often used in skipping. (1981,History of Children's Play,231)

O.B.¶

abbr. ordinary building [sawmillers' term meaning 'ordinary building' (rimu) as contrasted with heart wood]

WILSON There were good boards among these facecuts which today would be classed as O.B. (1955,Land of My Children,28)

O.M.¶

abbr. the overland mail (as distinct from that carried by sea) in the North Island

No less than a dozen native letters were received by yesterday's O.M. (1856,McLean Papers,3/10) [NOTES: J.Webster]

O.P. whisky¶

n. over-proof whisky

MASTERS Goods would have to be delivered at various places along the route, and often among the goods would be demijohns of O.P. whisky. (1960,Back-Country Tales,168)

STRONACH Fourteen musterers, two packmen, one cook (old Wattie), two rabbiters, one rouseabout, three gallons of O.P. whisky, one accordion ... [sic] mix well - and the result will surprise you! (1953,Musterer on Molesworth,16)

O.P.S.O.¶

abbr. On Public Service Only, used on mail from Government Departments sent overseas from 1891 - 1906 or on stamps identifying such mail. [ORIGIN: acronym]

Fold up your form, place it in an envelope, address it to the Registrar of Electors for Wairau, or Picton, as the case may be, at the top of the envelope write, "O.P.S.O. - Claim to Vote," which renders a postage stamp unecessary, and drop the letter into the nearest Post Office box. (Jun 9, 1887,Marlborough Express,3)

It is .. safe to assume that if any of the happy Liberals of this district .. receive from their President the message, "O.P.S.O. - Memo., urgent - take precedence - Attend without fail at Massey's meeting, and give him the worst turn he ever had", it will be accompanied by the police reminder that not even the back of the telegram should be used as the vehicle for the questions that are to shatter the reputation of the champion of "the selfish few". (May 4, 1905,Evening Post,4)

The Premier himself could hardly have made a better job of that telegram, yet it does not appear to have been sent O.P.S.O. - Memo, Urgent - Take Precedence." (Nov 18, 1905,Evening Post,4)

O.Pip¶

abbr. observation post [WW1]

TREADWELL ... the soldiers were using the spire as an O. Pip, or observation post. ... The corporal who had been using this O.P. preceded me up the spire. (1936,Recollections of an Amateur Soldier,110)

oak¶

n. an early name applied to various New Zealand trees thought to resemble the English oak either in appearance or in the quality of their timber; (espec Vitex lucens, PURIRI) [obs.]

YATE (1835,New Zealand (various authors/titles)) [NOTES: get quot.]

Puriri, which has been called the Oak of the Pacific, and the New Zealand Oak. (1840,NZ Journal,1/11/139)

DIEFFENBACH Of Vervenaceae (same order as the teak), the Vitex littoralis is the Puriri of the natives and the New Zealand oak of the colonists. (1843,Travels in NZ,1/431)

HOCHSTETTER Vitex lucens (1867,New Zealand (Hochstetter)) [NOTES: (list of trees from Taylor 1848)]

KIRK Vitex lucens (1889,Forest Flora)

LAING & BLACKWELL Vitex lucens (1906,Plants of NZ)

BROOKES The puriri is called the New Zealand oak. (1892,Frontier Life,174)

LAING & BLACKWELL Vitex lucens (The Puriri). A fine tree, from 50ft. to 60ft. in height, often called the New Zealand Oak, on account of the strength and durability of its timber. (1907,Plants of NZ,350,1940 p. 372 ed.)

HAY Vitex lucens (1915,Reminiscences of Earliest Canterbury)

MATTHEWS N.Z. Oak (Puriri), see Vitex lucens (1943,NZ Gardening Dictionary, The,128)

Oak (&obreve.k), n. (Bot.) [Add.] ... New Zealand oak. See under PURIRI. (1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2028)

The Puriri, or New Zealand Oak, or Teak, (Vitex littoralis,) is a large tree of irregular growth. (1869,TrNZI,1/III/43 () [NOTES: Colenso Botany North Isd]

BARLOW The Puriri (Vitex littoralis), sometimes called the New Zealand oak, is perhaps the next in importance to the Kauri ... It is principally used for railway sleepers, house blocks, ... and fencing posts. (1888,Kaipara and Waitemata Echo,114)

KEENE puriri: New Zealand oak, a large, handsome tree with hard timber. (1963,O Te Raki,194)

2 n. pohutukawa

POLACK The Puriri (Metrocideros florida or quercus Australis), is a wood whose durability equals any of the timbers in the country; it is very tough and close grained, and has been termed the Oak of the Pacific; but the Teak would be more appropriate, ... (1838,New Zealand (Polack),2/393) [NOTES: [some confusion here]]

BENNETT [The pohutukawa] is the New Zealand Oak and Fire-tree of the Europeans. (1860,Gatherings of a Naturalist,347)

HOCHSTETTER Metrosideros robusta New Zealand Oak-elm (1867,New Zealand (Hochstetter)) [NOTES: (list of trees from Taylor 1848)(A]

3 n. titoki, NZ ash.

MORRIS (1898,Austral English)

MATTHEWS Alectryon excelsum (1890,Descriptive & Priced List)

Alectryon excelsum (1916,Official Trade Register of Nurserymen)

4 n. mapau, lemonwood [1915 HEC. (Andersen)]

MORRIS Oak, n. The Oak of the Northern Hemisphere (Quercus) is not found among the indigenous trees of Australia; ... The word Oak is further extended to a few trees, not Casuarinae, given below; and in New Zealand it is also applied to Matipo(q.v.) and Titoki, or Alectryon(q.v.). (1898,Austral English,325)

DUNCAN & DAVIES Pittosporum eugenioides (1917,Descriptive Catalogue)

5 n.

BEGG (|= macron precedes) oak, e|nak [inaka or grass tree] (1979,Boultbee Journal,283) [NOTES: 1826-28 Boultbee Vocabulary in 1979 Begg]

oak - e|n|ak [inaka] (1835,Boultbee Journal) [NOTES: c1826-1827 list of Maori words in c1835 Boultbee Journal]

6 n.

Carpodetus serratus (1872,Catalogue of the Colonial and Vienna Exhibition)

7 n. ? kahikatea ? miro ? birch

McNABB Q. Are there many harbours there [on the west side of New Zealand]? A. Several ... All covered with wood Q. Of what sort? A. Of white and red pine and a wood called black oak'. Q. Is it of the species calledkaikatua'? A. It is. Q. Did you see any of the species called `cowrie'? A. I did not. (1908,Historical Records,1/559) [NOTES: 1821 May Evidence of McDonald before Commissioner Bigge in 1908 McNab Historical Records]

oak-weed¶

n.

MARTIN Oakweed or Oak-leafed Sea-wrack (Landsburgia quercifolia). The OAK-WEED belongs to yet another endemic genus with two distinct species, ... [It bears] much-lobed leaves shaped like those of the English oak. (1961,Flora NZ,16,1929 ed.)

Oamarooster¶

n.

His name it isn't Thomson, / And he is no Masseyite; / But a life-long loyal Liberal, / Though not much given to "skite." / A well-known Oamarooster / On whom none look askance, / A chap who has been uster / Much dealings with finance; / A one-time Seddon booster / Who made the Tories dance. (Oct 5, 1918,NZ Truth,6)

When "Critic" pictures some of the blacksmith shops he knows still "carrying on," that Oamarooster "smiddy" must be in a bad way indeed. (Oct 30, 1920,NZ Truth,1)

An Oamarooster tells of an interesting case of egg-vitality which came under his notice in London. (Oct 9, 1909,Observer (Auckland),16)

"OAMAROOSTERS" Would Like to Leave the Country [hdg] Berry and Brunie Board a Boat And Indulge in "Wholesale Bribery and Corruption" But are Landed by the "Lor" (Jul 7, 1917,NZ Truth,2)

Oamaru pudding¶

n.

McCREDIE ... The Bungalow recipe Book 51 OAMARU PUDDING ... milk ... gelatine ... sugar ... rind of 1 lemon ... yolks of 2 eggs ... soak ... gelatine ... in ... milk ... boil the rind tn the rest ... add soaked gelatine and sugar; stir in the yolks ... strain into wet shape; turn out when cold, and stick almonds over it. (1915,Bungalow Recipe Book, The)

Oamaru stone¶

n. a white building stone formerly much used. [from Oamaru in the South Island]

The church will cost GBP20,000 finished, being built of concrete with Oamaru stone facings and ornaments. (Feb 7, 1910,Colonist, The,2)

On sale at Greenfield and Stewarts Steam Saw Mills, Courtenay Place ... Tombstones of Oamaru stone, with blue stone base. (Jan 25, 1867,Evening Post,1)

[Hdg] Rotten Oamaru stone on Auckland City Town Hall. (Mar 13, 1926,NZ Observer,2)

The complex was partly built from Oamaru stone, in imitation of Indian sandstone. (Jun 10, 1992,Evening Post,23)

Oamaru, with its brilliant white buildings of Oamaru stone, can be seen a long way off on a sunny day. (Aug 11, 1938,NZ Observer,11)

I presume this formation has been generally considered to overlie immediatley the oamaru building stone. ...Interposed between the `Blue clay' and Oamaru stone, is a layer of sand ... (1870,TrNZI,2/168) [NOTES: Traill Oamaru and Moeraki]

BATHGATE (1874,Colonial Experiences,18)

VOGEL Oamaru Stone (1875,Official Handbook of NZ,107)

The most important building material that hitherto has been used in Otago. viz. the Oamaru Stone. The use of this material is coeval with the settlement of the district in which it occurs, but it was little known beyond until 1866, when an export trade commenced with Dunedin... The Oamaru stone, correctly speaking, is a white granular limestone. (1875,TrNZI,8/140) [NOTES: Blair `Building Materials in Otago']

HECTOR `A white, granular limestone, called the Oamaru stone, is worked in extensive quarries in the Oamaru district. ...A considerable quantity has been exported to Melbourne.' (1883,Handbook for New Zealand, The,64)

A white granular limestone called the Oamaru stone is worked in extensive quarries in the Oamaru district; ... The principal buildings of Dunedin are built of this stone, which shows a very fair amount of durability. (1892,NZ Official Handbook,197)

MORRIS Oamaru Stone, n. Oamaru is a town on the east coast of the South Island of New Zealand. It produces a fine building stone. (1898,Austral English,327)

There is abundance of good building stone [in South Canterbury], chiefly bluestone (dolomite), and white stone something like the famous Oamaru stone, ... (1900,Canterbury Old & New,129)

Oamaru stone was used in these constructions. (1914,History of North Otago,12,1978 ed.)

There are some men sawing Oamaru stone in Princes Street [for the university building]. (1923,Auckland Capping Book,16)

WALLIS He was squatting on his heels on the [Christchurch] balcony, the Oamaru stone balustrade level with his head. (1963,Point of Origin,108)

~ n. fine New Zealand building stone. [from name of place]. (1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),621)

As all the houses [sc. in Oamaru] are built of the peculiar, handsome Oamaru white stone, the appearance is very striking. (Sep 16, 1890,Otago Witness,34)

The Oamaru stone is perhaps the best known building stone in Otago. (Oct 4, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,15)

The property consists of a large and elegant stone store .. built of Oamaru stone, and after the most approved style of architecture. (Nov 2, 1865,North Otago Times,4)

The plans of the Presbyterian Church .. represent a building of the Gothic order, which, when finished in Oamaru stone, will show considerable architectural beauty. (Sep 1, 1864,Oamaru Times,1)

The material to be employed [on the Episcopal Parish Church of St John, Tokomairiro] is pressed bricks, made in the neighbourhood. Oamaru stone dressings will be employed about the windows, and bands of the same and black glazed bricks will run round the building. (Jan 27, 1866,Otago Witness,3)

The beauty of the Oamaru stone, the facility with which it can be worked, and its capability to with stand the action of the atmosphere, will ensure its being largely used for building purposes so soon as it becomes generally known throughout the colonies. (Jul 26, 1866,North Otago Times,3)

Oamaru stone in Melbourne. I think the quality of Oamaru stone to which I refer is a much superior building material to any of the Hobart Town stone that I have seen in this Province. (Nov 12, 1867,North Otago Times,2)

The chalk stone is white - like Oamarustone of New Zealand - only finer anddenser in structure. (Jan 2, 1918,Chronicles NZEF,III/34 : 19)

oarfish¶

n. Regalecus sp.

In November, 1902, I received a silvery Oar-fish, which was washed ashore ... near PortChalmers ... (1904,TrNZI,36/198)

HUTTON REGALECUS GLADIUS. Walb.? Oar Fish. (1872,Fishes,35, 56)

In shape, they are usually long, deep and very much compressed and flattened on thesides, so much so that their local appellations always embody some idea of these peculiarities - such as ribband-fish, lath or deal fish, band-fish or blade-fish, and also oar-fish. (1881,TrNZI,13/196) [NOTES: Clarke]

SHERRIN Regalecus gladius Walb. Oar-fish (1886,Handbook Fishes of NZ,303)

On its arrival in Christchurch ... I found the fish to be a species of Regalecus, or oar-fish of unusually large proportions. (1891,NZ Journal of Science,1/154)

On a Species of Regalecus or Great Oar-fish, caught in O'Kain's Bay.[title] (1892,TrNZI,24/192)

DRUMMOND & HUTTON The flesh of deep sea fishes, such as the ribbon fish and the oarfish, becomes a jelly when boiled, and is quite unfit to eat. (1902,Nature in NZ,74)

PHILLIPPS Regalecus pacificus Haast. OAR-FISH. (1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,26,1971 ed.)

SPEIGHT The Oar-fish, 12 to 20 feet long, with a high, flame-tipped head-crest (the elongated fin rays) is probably the sea-serpent of repute. Its pelvic fins are narrow, elongated processe, hence the name Oar-fish. The Deal-fish is much smaller, but of the same type, but we know very little of the habits of either of them. (1927,Natural History of Canterbury,198) [NOTES: Archey `Native Fishes of Canterbury' in Speight et al. Natural History of Canterbury]

RICHARDS [note to 1882 Oct 16 Found a peculiar fish stranded. 11ft. long ... Bright silver colour ...] Regalecus argentius, Oar fish, a deep sea fish. (1950,Chudleigh Diary,313)

GRAHAM Oarfish Regalecus pacificus Haast Syn. R. argenteus Parker) (1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,404) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

WILSON OARFISH A strange fish that is believed to have contributed to at least some of the sea serpent legends. The Oarfish belongs to the Ribbonfish family and is long, thin and extreamely fragile. (1981,Fisherman's Bible,170)

NATUSCH RIBBONFISHES: Order ALLOTRIOGNATHI ... Long, thin fishes of this group ... sometimes come [ashore.] [captions Fig. 65] 1. Oarfish, Regalecus ... 5. Ribbonfish, Agrostichthys. (1967,Animals of NZ, The,216)

AYLING OARFISH Regalecus glesne (Regalecus pacificus) ... It has been suggested that sightings of large oarfishes have been responsible for sailors' reports of sea serpents. (1982,Collins Guide,190,1984 ed.)

[species name] oarfish .. [scientific name] Regalecus glesne .. [other name(s)] -- (Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters)

PAUL Oarfish Regalecus glesne World-wide; ... (1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,69)

PAUL et al. [index to common names 283-293] oarfish 1088-1101 OARFISHES 1086-1101 (1987,Fishes: A Bibliography)

Mr. Travers gave an interesting account of several rare species of fish found on our coasts. Among them is Bank's oarfish. One of these was cast ashore at Nelson, and is now in the British Museum; it is of an extraordinary form, fifteen feet in length, twice that width [?], and almost as thin as the blade of an oar. (Sep 26, 1868,Otago Witness,13)

Mr Travers gave a very interesting account of several rare species of fish which had been found on our coasts, such as Bank's oarfish, one of these (the fourth of the kind ever obtained) was cast ashore at Nelson, and was now in the British Museum; it was of an extraordinary form, 15 feet in length, twice that width, and almost as thin as the blade of an oar. (Sep 17, 1868,Wellington Independent,3)

PHILLIPPS Regalecus argenteus Parker. PARKER'S OAR-FISH. (1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,26,1971 ed.)

2 n.

POWELL SILVERY OARFISH (Regalecus argenteus).This is a bizarre inhabitant of the ocean depths, which comes to the surface on rare occasions in New Zealand waters....On the under side near the head are two long feelers with broadened tips and from these it gets its popular name of oar-fish. Oar-fishes of several species are widely distributed.... (1947,Native Animals of NZ,65)

oatmeal water¶

n. brose, a refreshment for shearers or outdoor workers, a mixture of water, sugar and oatmeal.

[We] supped on the last three sheep's tongues, washed down with oatmeal water and the last drop of whisky. (Apr 7, 1883,Otago Witness,9)

OATMEAL WATER: A billy of water in which oatmeal and sugar is mixed is kept in the shearing shed. Plain water is inclined to have a weakening effect on anyone who is working hard. (1949,High Country Days,196)

HARTLEY After breakfast Dad left the house with mugs and a cream can of oatmeal water - a refreshing drink of rain water, lemon juice and a large handful of oatmeal. (1987,Swagger on Our Doorstep,188)

If very thirsty, drink a little tea (cold or hot) or half a pint of oatmeal water. (Feb 28, 1883,Tuapeka Times,6)

That oatmeal water is an excellent drink for people who are troubled with constipation. (Feb 20, 1907,Otago Witness,65)

A capital substitute for beer in hot weather ... boil the oatmeal water, stir thoroughly, when cold add the lime juice and sugar, and then use ... It is known as "Stokos", and is popular as the best drink for hard work. (Jan 30, 1886,Tuapeka Times,2)

To remove freckles from the face and hands try rubbing the juice of a lemon on at night, then in the morning to soften the skin bathe the face in oatmeal water. (Apr 1, 1887,Otago Witness,32)

To accomplish the work nearly 5000 men were employed, and they worked two successive days of 17 hours with only short intervals for means. The strain on men was exceptional, yet not beer but oatmeal and water was provided by the railway company ... This triumph of oatmeal over beer should not pass unnoticed. (Oct 11, 1892,Hawera & Normanby Star,2)

For very young infants, in lieu of mother's or nurse's milk, cream with barley, rice, or oatmeal water, to which milk, sugar, common salt, phosphate of lime, or sode [?], or limewater, in small quantities is added, seems best. (May 7, 1890,Tuapeka Times,4)

The honor carried with it the all-too-severe sentence of two years' reformative treatment at Pakatoa, where shikkers are herded together to cultivate a taste for nothing stronger than milk shake and oatmeal water. (Jun 20, 1914,NZ Truth,2)

When you have heavy or tiring work to do, do not take either Beer or spirits. By far the best drink is thin oatmeal water, with a little sugar. (Jan 28, 1888,Te Aroha News,1)

Oceania¶

n. Australia, New Zealand and the Pacific Islands

Big international corporates are now beginning to use the nearly 200-year old term "Oceania" to refer to their Australia, New Zealand and Pacific Islands operations. The very word "Oceania" makes this seem like a much more attractive part of the world than "Australasia" does; it glistens with moisture, just as a seashell or rock under water is more tantalisingly pretty than when it is dried off and disappointingly dull. (Oct 28, 2000,Listener (New Zealand Listener),27)

odd-bod¶

n. 1.a person who cannot be classified into a social or personal type; someone of no fixed classification; no certain type; `odd-man-out'

n. 1.a person who cannot be classified into a social or personal type; someone of no fixed classification; no certain type; `odd-man-out'

Are you a type' or anodd-bod'? It was several days before I fathomed this out. The cry would go up, Ruapehu types arriving, get cracking.' The best club cups would be sorted out, an official or prominent member would greet the new-comers show them proudly over the house and dispense tea and hospitality with a cordial air. Then I'd hear,Odd bods coming up the track'. A member would supervise his arrival, see that no one entered the club-room with nailed boots on, and offer a cup of tea, but there was not that ostentatious display of club china, eats' and cordiality. I found that atype' is a member of a recognised club, and therefore someone to be treated with respect. An odd bod' is also a tramper or mountaineer, but of no specified breed, though entitled to shelter. The same law holds for bush and mountain: hospitality must be dispensed for folk of the right ilk, so that the billy is always on the boil. But tourists ! Well, I'd just hate to be a tourist ! They were treated with the loftiest contempt. I suppose I came under the headingodd bod' vouched for by a `type'. ... (Jun 14, 1951,NZ Woman's Weekly,24)

OVENDEN O.E., as every New Zealander knows, means Overseas Experience. The term refers, as in such colloquial phrases as: He's gone to get his O.E., eh,'; orGot your O.E. yet then?' to that most rare of concepts, an abstract consumer durable. It has little in common with the Australian vernacular O.S.', which is a mere descriptive term, as inHe's gone O.S. and won't come back.' (1986,O.E.,6)

O.E. falls into a tangle [heading] In his new novel O.E. Keith Ovenden returns to the basic elements of 1984's Ratatui ... Despite the bludgeoning conclusiveness of its title O.E. leaves a great deal unsaid about the strange place overseas experience holds in our cultural texture. (Nov 1, 1986,Dominion,5)

RICHARDSON She was to spend six months in London. `O.E. at last,' she said. (1986,Choices,64)

We had our formal educations, our OE, our 2.5 children and we were mortgaged, it seemed, for ever. (May 1987,Metro,62) [NOTES: Roger `Auckland Life']

McGILL OE overseas experience, the young Kiwi's working holiday abroad, mandatory and meaningful; coined by John Muirhead and popularised in late 1970s by friend and columnist Tom Scott; in 1980s, title of Keith Ovenden novel. (1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,78)

Most of my friends are either post-OEers - full of tales from afar - or ante-OEers - full of tales about what they're going to do when they go afar... But these trips don't cut the mustard as far as serious OEing goes. (Feb 6, 2000,Sunday Star Times,D2)

Suddenly it seemed everyone under 25 had done a couple of years of what became known as OE. (Mar 2004,North & South,65)

He met Lesley in Scotland. when he was over there "doing my OE", and they started out as sharemilkers in Te Puke. (Nov 1992,North & South,90)

Camilla Bellich says student debt has turned OE into "a money-making venture as opposed to a holiday or a life-experience. . . ." (Nov 2005,North & South,38)

One is this very New Zealand thing of going out in our 20s, like to war, or OE, and seeing these extraordinary places, but actually ending up finding out more about ourselves. (Nov 21, 1998,Listener (New Zealand Listener),36)

Three years ago she did her OE in somewhat unsual style, starting with South America. (May 5, 1984,Listener (New Zealand Listener),44)

The great OE, the New Zealand rite of passage, tattooed in our psyche, embedded in our genes. Young New Zealanders have been setting off on their OEs for over half a century. Ibid. 33 "OE has become an almost obligatory rite of cultural consumerism... For a middle-class young Pakeha New Zealander, not taking an OE is now unusual." (Apr 12, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),32)

I found on my brief OE that Englishmeneverywhere warmed appreciably when I wasable to prove I wasn't from Queensland. (Aug 21, 1976,Listener (New Zealand Listener),12)

SCOTT We are both suffering from what the richcall a jaded palate, others call"Cultural Imperialism" and what mysophisticated friends call OE depression.OE, of course, is the abbreviation foroverseas experience - and no NewZealander, it is claimed, is completewithout it. As well as being vital toemotional and intellectual (and sexual)development, OE very nicely fills thatawkward gap between high school andmarriage. (1977,Tom Scott's Life & Times,76)

Now the average Kiwi going on his or her OE is older and, arguably, smarter. Far from funding their OE working in a London pub, they're more likely to work in an office... Auckland's Jess Startup, 24, who went overseas in 1998, believes the pub-working OE is a thing of the past for most New Zealanders... For example, going to teach English in Korea or Japan is a lucrative way of having a Big OE. (Aug 23, 2003,Dominion Post,F1)

Horror of horrors, there are returning OErs among us who are smarting from the fact that their visas expired, or that they ran out of money in Pisa, or Florence, or Rome, or that they had to come home for a family wedding and can't afford to get away again. Adams) When a bettor through the T.A.B. bets on a double and the horse on the second leg of the double is scratched after the bet is laid the favourite at the tote on the course is substituted in the bet for the scratched horse. offsider¶

n.

REED The cleared land would then be ploughed with one or two pairs of bullocks, and though the ploughman would not have the assistance of a driver ... the offsider would not put a foot out of the furrow from end to end. (1953,Story of the Kauri, The,184) [NOTES: (D.J.McLeod)]

n.

And up lumbered the heavily-laden waggon with its driver, "offsider," and twelve horses. (Dec 24, 1897,Bruce Herald,6)

They went as what is called 'offsiders,' to drive spare bullocks, round up the bullocks straying from camp, flog up any lagging offside bullock, and generally to be useful in forming camp, cooking, and chopping wood, and drawing water - work such as the scarecrow boy from your hedgerow could do just as well. (Aug 1, 1899,Star, The,2)

The bullocky's offsider was the chap who helped the bullock driver, and the origin of the name `offsider' came about this way; very often when a team is operating it is necessary for someone to run round on the off side and push or force point bullocks over quickly ... for instance when going round a corner or avoiding some obstacle ... also to give general help. (1951,Radio NZ / National Radio) [NOTES: [a1914] 1951 H.S.McCarroll The Days of the Kauri Bushmen [Radio Talk 3: TS]]

REED The bullock-driver usually had an assistant called an offsider. ... At one time I engaged an intelligent young Maori, a likeable, willing fellow, as off-sider. (1953,Story of the Kauri, The,184) [NOTES: (D.J. McLeod)]

MOORE [Northland c 1920] The team was hitched to the rear of the waggon, and the Maori `offsider', a somewhat irresponsible [43] youth was instructed to guide the two polers .... (1969,From Forest to Farm,42)

1 n. (Rural) [sp. use] an assistant to a station or shearing gang cook.

STUDHOLME At Waimate there were about 50 men and boys employed for the shearing .. 1 cook's mate, generally known as the offsider, or 'slushy.' (1940,Te Waimate,127)

HAMILTON If there were more than a certain number of men to be provided for, the cook was given an assistant, generally known as his 'off-sider.' (1952,History of Northern Southland,45)

NEWTON Both the old packie and the cook's 'off-sider' were well past cricketing days. (1949,High Country Days,59)

MCLEOD One classer, two rollers .. make up the shed contingent and the cook and 'offsider' complete the total. (1951,NZ High Country,27)

2 n. (See slushy)

SMYTH The Maori boy who acted as the cook's `offsider'. (1929,Girl from Mason Creek, The,206)

Off-sider.- Assistant. Now mostly applied to the slushy, or cook's mate, but the term may have originated with the old bullock drivers, who drove their teams from the near side. I have oftenhelped the Glentanner bullock driver across the Tasman, by riding on the off side with a whip to keep the bullocks up stream; and he always asked if I would `off-side' for him. I have heard a bullocky speak of his dog as his o.s. (Jan 27, 1934,Press, The,15)

STUDHOLME Many of the old [c 1880s] station cooks, with an offsider (mate) .. Ibid. 91 Many of the old station cooks, with the assistance of an offsider' (mate) found no difficulty in cooking for fifty or sixty men. ... it has to be remembered that ... bread cost only 6d. or 7d. a 4lb. loaf, and tea (post and rails) could be bought for 1/- per lb. Some of these contract cooks were great fossickers and managed to find quite a lot of green stuff for vegetables ... <i>Ibid.</i> 127 At Waimate there were about 50 men and boys employed for the shearing - 22 shearers, 1 sheep-oh, 4 fleece-pickers, 4 wool-rollers, 1 brander, 1 wool-classer, 1 classer's boy, 3 pressers, 1 waggoner, 1 cook, 1 cook's mate, generally known as the offsider orslushy' 8 shepherds; and last, but not least the shed boss, ... [fn] In the early days the manager acted as shed boss. Later, when the agricultural work increased, a special shed boss was engaged. (1940,Te Waimate,75)

BRUNO [The cook] hearing of the unexpected arrival of a number of reinstouchments snarled to his offsiders. (1944,Desert Daze,49)

Oh One¶

n. a skinhead

The term Oh One for a skinhead has an interesting derivation; it comes from their characteristic expression 'Oi Oi' used as a greeting. When written in capitals OI OI (for example, as in the case of tattoos) it loooks like the numerals 'zero one' or 'oh one'. (Oct 2000,NZWords,2(2): 2)

Of these, 'bush-lawyer', cabbage tree and ohoho bloom only at the same time as pukapuka and all three yield more nectar than pukapuka does. (Jul 1911,NZ Farmer,765)

ohu¶

n. (Rural) a government-sponsored rural commune, usually on marginal land.

n.

Some went into communes - remember the ohu movement of the Kirk Government, of which Amos was a minister? (Sep 27, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),24)

HAYWARD [fn. to a 1.12.73 entry on Mr Kirk's interest in supporting alternative lifestyles] * The Listener in 1979 published a feature on the kibbutz or ohu scheme which described it as originating from a`chance remark by Norman Kirk.' (1981,Diary of the Kirk Years,183fn)

n. formerly, a state-sponsored, rural commune. -->a kibbutz-type of landed-community introduced by the Labour Government in the 1970s; a communal work group [ORIGIN: Maori]

KOHERE The work was performed by what was calledan "ohu" or working-bee. Only in thiscase the whole community or sub-tribeformed the "ohu." (1949,Story of a Maori Chief, The,28)

The pioneering spirit. It stops not to ask if that wilderness actually belongs to someone else, or whether one's 60s girlfriend really wants to live in a teepee and wash nappies in the river on some ohu. (Feb 12, 1994,Listener (New Zealand Listener),63)

WILLIAMS Ohu ... n. company of volunteer workers. (1871,Dictionary of the Maori Language,100,3rd ed.)

A Colac Bay native narrated that the late Rawiri te Awha had been one of a party of Maoris living on the shores of Te Anau some eighty or ninety years ago. Under the custom of ohu the men were assembled assisting a man to amke a garden at some distance from the whare (houses). (Apr 1921,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,4/88) [NOTES: Beattie]

The impression I gathered by personal inspection of some of the lands and from representations made at crowded meetings in seven settlements, where we met some 3000 of the northern tribes, was that they were doing more than any other section of the race - not excepting the East Coast - for themselves. Road-making on a 50/50 basis with the local bodies, ohu or mahi-apu [ed fn: A group of volunteer workers] in relation to scrub-cutting, bush felling, splitting posts and battens, gave ample evidence of self-help. (May 22, 1930,Na To Hoa Aroha,2/34,1987 ed.) [NOTES: Gnata]

HAYWARD Arthur Faulkner, still Acting Minister of Lands, has announced that the proposed communes will be called ohu' - a Maori word meaning to achieve somethingby means of friendly help and work.' (Mar 7, 1974,Diary of the Kirk Years,223,1981 ed.)

THREE OHUS There are three ohus on Crown land with a total area of 375 hectares, ... (Nov 2, 1977,Evening Post,2/7)

Ohu is a Maori term which refers to either a communal or volunteer work group. ...The main objective of the settlement was to offer an alternative way of life, and the Government decided tolease Crown land to groups. At the outset 38 groups were approved as eligible but only three have established themselves on Crown land. (1981,Dominion,13)

KING [caption] The ohu, or working bee, which cleared and sowed the Panguru block. By 1932, groups like this were bringing Maori land into production all over the North Island. (1988,Apirana Ngata)

[Ahu Ahu on the Wanganui River] was founded only 13 years ago, back in those heady Kirk years when the late Prime Minister gave the flower-power mob the land they yearned t return to.There they could build their communes. Ohu, he called them. This was one of eight ohu established and remains as one of only two that have seen the distance. (Dec 30, 1988,Dominion,11)

They went there in 1972, at the time newly elected Labour Prime Minister Norman Kirk was promoting his plan for State-sponsored communes or ohus, with the idea of finding a suitable property and dropping out for a while. (May 31, 2001,Evening Post,5)

Norm Kirk's reforming newly-elected Labour government's policies included support for communes, called ohu in the new-found enthusiasm for the native language. (Jan 7, 2003,Dominion Post,C1)

New Zealand's last remaining Ohu - a commune of alternative lifestylers established in the 70s, on state land given away to the flower power generation by the Norman Kirk Labour Government - survives further downstream. (Oct 2, 2000,Evening Post,13)

SINCLAIR & HARREX The Labour Government of 1972-5 assisted groups of people to form ohu - communes which were supposed to express Maori co-operative values. (1978,Looking Back,67)

The grey-faced petrel, or sombre shearwater - ornithological name Puffinus tristus, has the Maori names oi, oioi and oii; Cook's petrel (Procellaria Cooki), is titi; brown petrel (Procellaria parkinsoni) is taiko; Gould's petrel (Procellaria Gouldi) is ohi; rain bird (Puffinus gavius) is hokoakoa; (Puffinus assimilis) is totorore. The most common name in the south is titi, or oii; the others are seldom used. (Apr 20, 1903,Southland Times,2)

oioi¶

n. (Environment) a jointed wire rush plant, Apodasmia similis. [ORIGIN: Maori, Formerly classified Leptocarpus similus]

Leptocarpus simplex, Br. ... [266] Nat. name, `Oioi' (shaking), Col. (1853,Flora NZ,I Flowering Plants 265)

WILSON Leptocarpus similis JOINTED WIRE RUSH ... Oioi ... The Maori name oioi also means to shake or agitate; watch the beds of jointed wire rush when waves wash into them. (1982,Stewart Island Plants,324)

CROWE The jointed zigzag stems of oioi were gathered by early Maori for use as an outer thatching for homes... Oioi means 'to shake gently', which derives from the swaying of the stems as the wind blows through them. (1995,Which Coastal Plant?,51)

Oioi is an attractive and unusual plant with cylindrical stems and leaves that form a short sheath at intervals along the stem creating noticeable joints - hence the common name, jointed wire rush. (Aug 5, 2006,Dominion Post Indulgence,19)

OK¶

adj. as an adjective attested early in NZ (1865, Dunedin) and has remained a common feature of NZ speech; also in various forms espec. among children (OKE, OKIE, OKIE-DOKIE, OKIE-DOKE, DOKE). She'll be doke' would be more likely to be heard thanShe'll be OK.', as a by-form of `She'll be right'. spg also okay

The measures you have taken for lining the public purse are quite O.K. (Nov 11, 1865,Dunedin Punch,168)

I would include among New Zealand English words: O.K. (May 6, 1952,VUC First Year English Class)

[Two-up's] a real open air game - just the O.K. for a race-course. OEDS 1839 [derivation from orl korrect; thence Old Kinderhook 1840] (Mar 1912,Magpie, The)

Okareka Ash¶

n. Geol. definition

The base of the section comprises ridges of pumice sand gravel mantled by Okareka Ash, Rerewhakaaitu Ash and Rotorua Ash now truncated by lake waters when the lake level was at an elevation of 284 m as against the present day elevation of 279.7 m. (May 1975,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,39/35)

Okarito brown kiwi¶

n.

Young Okarito brown kiwi remain with their parents for at least nine months. (Jan 2000,NZ Geographic,No. 45/91)

[Operation Nest Egg] was conceived with the Okarito brown kiwi in mind, but because its population was so low - estimated to be around 150 in the early 1990s - it was decided to trial ONE on the more common North Island brown. (Jul 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 64/51)

Okarito brown kiwi are New Zealand's rarest kiwi and are on the nationally critical list. (Jan 10, 2007,NZ Herald,A7)

In 2002, the government decided to keep most of the remaining unsold land at Okarito in public hands, winning an extra habitat for one of New Zealand's rarest birds, the Okarito brown kiwi, and a sanctuary for a delicate ecology. (Feb 21, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),26)

Okarito forest gecko¶

n. brownish gecko with orange blotches, seldom encountered, restricted to small area in south Westland. [Hoplodactylus "Okarito forest gecko" aka Okarito gecko]

The confirmed lizard fauna of the Franz Josef/Waiau area comprises just fourspecies.the forest gecko, the Okarito forest gecko (presently confirmed from asingle specimen), the speckled skink, and the common skink. (2004,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.doc.org.nz/upload/documents/science-and-technical/WClizards.pdf]

Okarito Forest Gecko . . . A new species of Hoplodactylus granulatus first seen in the 1990s and recently confirmed as a distinct species using DNA sequencing but still to be described. (2009,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.doc.govt.nz/conservation/native-animals/reptiles-and-frogs/species-information/atlas-of-the-amphibians-and-reptiles-of-nz/electronic-atlas/atlas-details/?SpeciesID=13252]

Okataina Tephra¶

n. Geol. definition

On field appearance and stratigraphy, the thin tephra was considered not to be an Okataina tephra [sic, small 't'] (Kaiharoa or Whakatane Tephra) but perhaps a tephra from Taupo or the basaltic Rotokawau Ash. (Jan 1984,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,66/18)

okeoke¶

n. dogfish

LEE & KENDA;; O'ke |oke, s. A certain fish. (1820,Grammar & Vocabulary of the Language of NZ,141)

PHILLIPPS Squalus fernandinus Molina. SPINED DOGFISH; OKEOKE or KOINGA. (1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,9,1971 ed.)

SPINY DOGFISH. Squalus fernandinus , Molina. The spiny dogfish (Squalus fernandinus ) is one of those species of shark which has attained importance as an economic species. ... It is generally called oke-oke, or kapeta, by [Maori]; the term pioke is applied by some tribes to this species, while to other tribes pioke is any small species of shark. (1929,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,11/223) [NOTES: Phillipps]

Squalus fernandinus Molina. Spined dogfish. Okeoke. (1938,TrRSNZ,68/401) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

GRAHAM SPINED DOGFISH (KOINGA OR OKEOKE) Koinga lebruni (Vailliant) ...Spined Dogfish is so named from the single spine in front of each dorsal fin, and Dogfish probably because of its supposed carnivorous habits. There are several species of small sharks that are known as Dogfish. [ibid. 82]. The Maori name for Spined Dogfish is Koinga or Okeoke, and the fish is used by them as food, usually being hung outside and dried, when it will keep for long periods. (1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,80,2nd ed.)

DOOGUE & MORELAND NORTHERN DOGFISH ... Other names: Squalus blainvillii; Griffin's dogfish, common spined dogfish, brown dogfish; pioke, okeoke (Maori). (1966,Sea Anglers' Guide,186)

n. in general (non-Otago) use, occasionally with some development of meaning or application. The first element of the collocation, `old', is not always felt as inseparable from the second, allowing a descriptive term (usually a local or place-name) to be inserted. -->a. a person long identified with a place, position, institution, etc; an oldinhabitant

THATCHER This Auckland's getting civilized,- / Victorians come this way,/ And show these Old Identities / The proper time o'day./ (1864,Songs of the War,15)

The `old identities' as the older residents [of Auckland] are termed. (1865,Evening Post,1/53/2)

CLYDE A penniless new chum was I, / A wealthy carle was he; / One of those ancient ones, y clept / The Old Identity. (1872,Te Kooti,37)

PRESHAW [Diary dated 5 Feb 1864] The police - Old Nelson identities - were quite unprepared for any such emergencies. (1888,Banking Under Difficulties,124)

D'AVIGDOR To blow' is, in the colonies, to brag or boast; this is graphic if not elegant; but one of the most curious expressions, based on an entirely errroneous derivation, isan old identity', used for a person long `identified with a place' - in fact an old inhabitant, or one whose interests have long been bound up in it. This expression is unfortunately now universal even in newspapers. (1888,Antipodean Notes,171)

WILSON A mixed gathering, containing some members of the class who in this country are known as OldIdentities', but whom I heard an aged man designate, with a lapsus linguae, that had in it much unconscious irony:Old Nonentities'. (1894,In the Land of the Tui,256) [NOTES: 1892 Jan 16 [Wakatipu] in 1894 Wilson Land of the Tui]

MORRIS Old Identity phrase denoting a person well known in a place. A term invented in Dunedin, New Zealand, in 1862, in a popular topical song, by Mr. R. Thatcher, an improvisator. In the song the Old Identity,' the former resident of Dunedin, was distinguished from theNew Iniquity,' as the people were termed who came from Australia. [1879. W. J. Barry, `Up and Down,' p. 197:] (1898,Austral English,209)

Old identities are dying all over the Colony. (Mar 31, 1906,NZ Truth,41-1-3)

BATHGATE [Old Identity] was the name applied by the people of Victoria, who flocked hither in thousands on the discovery of gold, to those who had been in the province before that time. Those honest folks, the tenour of whose ways had previously been very even, were greatly started by this immense and sudden influx of population. 16,000 persons landed in three months, and everythig went up to worse than famine prices. It is alleged that one minister actually prayed that the stream of rogues and vagabonds which was flowing into the country might be stayed, but this I believe to have been a base invention of the new iniquity', as the new-comer were nicknamed. The termold identities' took its origin from an expression in a speech made by one of the members of the Provincial Council, Mr. E.B. Cargill, who, in speaking of the new arrivals, said that the early settlers should endeavour to preserve their old identity. The strangers, who were inclined to laugh at the aboriginals as a set of old stagers, caught up the phrase, and dubbed them old identities'. Acomic singer helped to perpetuate the name by writing a song the refrain of which was something in this style -Touch not that shaky jetty, / Let that old post-office be, / That's one way to preserve / Your old identity'. So that anything effete came to be styled a regular old identity'. So much did the name come to be a term of reproach among the Australian diggers that the barracouta 'thyrsites atum), a rather coarse fish, very abundant at certain seasons, and which was consequently very cheap, and a great favourite with boarding-house keepers, was christened by themthe old identity' (1874,Colonial Experiences,25)

REEVES The canny Scotch old identity' was swallowed up in thenew iniquity', as they mutually styled each other. (1892,Homeward Bound,3)

HOCKEN The origin of the descriptive term Old Identities' has been involved in some doubt. Not only is it a convenient one, but it is now widely used throughout the Colonies to [202] denote the oldest inhabitants, as distinguished from the newcomers, ornew iniquities.' Mr. Charles R. Thatcher, who was the son of a natural history collector at Brighton, and was about his father's business, came to Dunedin with the great rush in February, 1862, and gave vocal entertainments which were highly relished. He was a clever improviser, and nightly sang in rough verse to delighted audiences the events of the day, which then, though trivial, were numerous enough. Mr. E. B. Cargill was seeking a seat in the Council, and addressing the electors, regretted that the old settlers were being thrown into the shade, and that to prevent this they must make a strong and united effort. This was food for Thatcher, who thereupon brought down the house with a witty song of eight verses, each of which terminated with the refrain of The Old Identity.' Thus:-Does he wish each brother Scotchman/ To come out in a kilt - / To kittle up the chanter,/ And go in for a lilt?/ With bare legs in Otago/ How very cold 'twould be!/ But it's one way of preserving/ The Old Identity.' Yes, it did seem as though the new iniquity' were, like a flowing tide, to obliterate theold identity.' But no such catastrophe happened. (1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,201)

SINCLAIR Dunedin became the largest town in the country, while, to the horror of the Old Identity', saloons, billiard rooms, gambling dens and dance halls sprang up to attract the gold of theNew Iniquity'. (1959,History of New Zealand,104)

old man snapper¶

n. [[used with fish freq.]] (See snapper)

Calculus is a fine upstanding son of Trenton and Rosemary, with the most pronounced Roman nose I have ever seen an animal display; in fact it forcibly put me in mind of an old man schnapper. (Oct 27, 1892,Otago Witness,28)

GRAHAM Many large Snapper are caught with a peculiar hump on the head which is caused by a thickening of the frontal bone. When a fisherman lands such a fish he immediately states: I've caught an old man Snapper' under the erroneous impression it is an old male fish. I have often dissected theseold man' Snappers and found that there were as many female as male fish with this conspicuous bony knob. The name `old man Snapper' comes from the likeness to the face of an old man with bulbous red nose, a short clipped moustache and tight upper lip, which can be seen in the illustration. (1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,245)

WILSON Old man snapper' are those fish which develop a hump on top of the head as they become older; often the fleshy upper lip also increases in size. This condition may affect females as well as males. The termold man may also be applied to old, slabby fish in poor condition, whether they have developed a hump or not. (1981,Fisherman's Bible,227)

old man snapper (Feb 1981,Avondale College Slang Words in Use) [NOTES: (Goldie Brown Collection)]

old man's beard¶

n. a rampant, troublesome introduced clematis, a strangler of trees and shrubs, whose flowers are replaced in autumn by dense grey-white down. [(cf OLD MAN'S WHISKER(S); TRAVELLERS' JOY) (old man's whisker is also WILD FENNEL)]

Old man's beard is a member of the clematis family, and was introduced from Europe as a decorative plant... "This is a good time to look out for old man's beard. It tends to spill out of hedges at this time of year, or you might see it flowering in the tops of trees or along roadsides." (Mar 5, 2002,Independent Herald (Wellington),17)

Waving his hands about like David Bellamy stumbling unexpectedly upon a fresh crop of old man's beard, he passes on a fresh secret of business success. (May 12, 1995,Listener (New Zealand Listener),64)

a wild clematis in its winter form, clothing hedgerows. (1979,Heinemann NZ Dictionary,746)

TAYLOR TRAVELLER'S JOY (Clematis vitalba) ... In autumn dense down takes the place of flowers . . ., giving the alternative name `old man's beard'. ... Traveller's joy was introduced from Europe but extends into native forest. (1981,Weeds of Roadsides,149)

The fast-growing introduced vine Old Man's Beard is smothering native bush in many parts of the city and surrounding hills, ... Also known as Clematis vitalba, Traveller's Joy and Mile-a-Minute, the climber spreads rapidly. (May 8, 1984,Karori and Western Suburbs News,8)

5 Old man's beard is raging through native bush and may be stopped only by public concern. -->The Department of Lands and Survey is holding an old man's beard seminar in Wellington in November, detailing the widespread destruction the vine has caused in native bush. (Sep 13, 1982,Evening Post)

old woman from Botany Bay¶

n.

SUTTON-SMITH The Dialogue game of Caricature includes The Old Lady from Botany bay. In this game the children must not laugh at the old lady although she does her best to provoke them to do so. It has been known as Diggley Bones (Auckland [19]00), An Old Man from B.B. (Palmerston [19]10), An Old Lady from Poverty bay (Wellington [19]00). It is widely reported in the last twenty years. The Old Lady comes up to the children and says `Here comes the Old Lady from Botany Bay, and what [433]have you got to give her today. You must neither laugh nor smile but say ----'. Then the child would say something funny but try not to laugh when doing so. ... (Nelson [19]49). (1953,Unorganized Games,2/432)

Omahina tephra¶

n. Geol. defn.

Another widespread silicic airall tephra reported from southern North Island is the Rangitawa Pumice and its possible correlatives, the Omahina Tephra (Pillans 1979), the Lower Finnie Road Ash (Seward 1974 ; Pillans & Kohn 1981), and the Mangaroa Ash (Neeser et al. 1980), all with fission-track dates in the two-standard deviation age range of 370 +- 70 ky BP (Pillans & Kohn 1981; Kyle & Seward 1984; Kohl 1986, oral presentation). (Nov 1988,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,82/36)

Ombudsman¶

n. an officer of Parliament appointed by the Legislature to investigate complaints by individuals against the state or public services. [Swedish, legal representative]

TURNER In July 1961 he was to be called the `Parliamentary Commissioner for Investigation'.... The office was modelled on the Scandinavian ombudsman and this word was used in the meantime ... . The Plural ombudsmen appeared, and a man checking hotel measures was called a drinker'sombudsman In 1962 the name ombudsman was officially adopted. (1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,174)

(Jun 8, 1966,Weekly News,10/2) [NOTES: OEDS]

(Sep 28, 1966,NZ News,3/2) [NOTES: OEDS]

HILL Although the Ombudsman has the power to be self-activating, nearly all of his investigations are initiated by citizens' complaints. Hence, it is scarcely an exaggeration to say that the Ombudsman's role is largely determined by his complaints. (1976,Model Ombudsman,79)

It has recommended a small change in the title from Parliamentary Commissioner for Investigations Bill to Parliamentary Commissioner (Ombudsman) Bill. The reason is that the public is not talking about the Parliamentary Commissioner for Investigations but about the appointment of an ombudsman. (1962,NZ Parliamentary Debates,331/1758)

I have no proposals to bring before the government for the appointment of a consumers ' ombudsman. (1972,NZ Parliamentary Debates,381/3558)

It is proposed to extend the jurisdiction of the Ombudsman to all local authorities. (1973,NZ Parliamentary Debates,382/10)

The term "ombudsman" has general acceptance and has passed into common usage. The reservations that existed 12 years ago over using an unfamiliar and foreign term are no longer supportable. (1974,NZ Parliamentary Debates,395/5735)

There will be a Chief Ombudsman who will be the prime Ombudsman. (1974,NZ Parliamentary Debates,395/5737)

There shall be appointed, as an officer of Parliament, a Commissioner for investigations, to be called the Ombudsman. (1962,NZ Statutes,10/107)

It shall be a function of the Ombudsman to investigate any decision or recommendation made.. relating to a matter of administration and affecting any person or body of persons in his or its personal capacity, in or by any of the Departments or organisations named or specified in.. the First Schedule of this Act. (1975,NZ Statutes,9/98)

on drop/on the drop¶

Heifers on drop have been worth GBP14 to GBP18 10/-. (Sep 1, 1949,NZ Farmer,24)

Cows on drop were worth up to GBP22, heifers on drop GBP18 to GBP23. (Oct 25, 1951,NZ Farmer,34)

You'll notice that ewes heavy in lamb and 'on-the-drop' slow up a lot. (2002,Lifestyle Farmer,June/July 54)

on gold¶

phr. working a payable claim.

... but after all there are only 11 claims on gold ... (Jan 9, 1869,Grey River Argus,3)

And there's a whole mob of fellows shepherding them [to discover their claim]. (1873,Wild Will Enderby,3/i/75,1889, 1974 ed.)

It was first stated that every claim was on gold; the poorest paying [pounds] 40 per week,... (Mar 2, 1867,West Coast Times) [NOTES: 1867 Mar 2 West Coast Times in 1985 Bromby Eye Witness History 49]

They've struck a big old placer somewhere, you bet. They are on heavy gold too. (1873,Wild Will Enderby,Bk III ch i 75,1889 ed.)

MILLER I was on gold all the time. I had sunk my first prospect hole at the lower end of a shallow depression ... and had struck the deepest part of the lead. It was a narrow gutter, no more than afoot wide at the most, and rich in heavy gold. (1946,There Was Gold,69)

on one's pat¶

phr. on one's own, alone

ANTHONY ... a boy along to help Gus on the stack, but he said `No', a man should be able to build a round stack on his pat, and he didn't want any boy standing about in the way while he was working. (1927,Me and Gus,73) [NOTES: a1927 Anthony Me and Gus (1977 Gus Tomlins ed. Sturm) 73]

PARTRIDGE Pat (or Pat), on one's. Alone; single-handed: Australian and New Zealand: C. 20. C. J. Dennis, 1916. Ex Pat Malone, q.v. .. Pat. 2. (Gen. pl.) A Chinaman: New Zealand c. (- 1932). Prob. suggested by the relevant sense of John. (1937,Dictionary of Slang,609)

HYDE It's tough if he's got a wife and kids somewhere.'Why? If he was enjoying himself on his pat, why wasn't that just as hard?' Ibid. 187 She [the river] ain't used to it [sc. dam-building]. She's been going along on her pat for a long time now. Ibid. 214 The fact is, I'm a stranger here and on my pat. (1938,Nor the Years Condemn,124)

SARGESON But why not stay the night, Dave? Mr Anderson went on. Don't leave a man all on his pat. (1949,I Saw in My Dream,216,1974 ed. Rhodes ed.)

SYDER & HODGETTS (said, ON ME PAT';) Alone. .in 4;I can travel faster by myself - I'll be on me pat today'. (1974,Australian & NZ English [TS],813) [NOTES: (1962-63)1974 Syder and Hodgetts Aust. and NZ English [TS] 813]

SARGESON pat: all on my pat, by myself. (1964,Collected Stories,301)

pat, on one's (colloq. phrase). alone, on one's own, shortened form of `on one's Pat Malone.' (1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),622)

pat, on one's (Coll.phr.) alone [Rhyming slang Pat Malone]. (1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),15)

SLATTER Good old Johnny ... Remember him getting two tries all on his pat at Castelraimodo? (1970,On the Ball,135)

McGILL pat, phr. on one's pat single-handed or alone, short for rhyming slang on one's Pat Malone/alone' or on yourown'; eg `Since Mabel passed on, Ron's been on his pat for too much. Time we went round and jollied him up, fellas. Agree?' ANZ. (1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,82)

I'll stick on me belt and gas bag, and buzz off to the village on me pat. (Jan 2, 1918,Chronicles NZEF,III/34: 13)

on terms¶

1 phr. (Rural) [sheep] a long term grazing arrangement where sheepowner and landowner share profit of wool sales.

HOBHOUSE Flocks are often let on Terms ... (Nov 4, 1859,Letter)

ACLAND In 1856 Porter took the run and sheep on term ... (1946,Early Canterbury Runs,102)

PAUL Sheep may be placed out in the same manner, on terms ... (1857,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,46)

Everyone suggests different investments. One says buy sheep and put them out on terms. (1860,BLANK ENTRY,39) [NOTES: in BUTLER First Year (1863)]

BALFOUR Under this arrangement, a landless man, who had enough capital to buy sheep, could graze them on another man's property. Usually, the grazier took one-half of the annual wool clip of the 'terms' sheep and one-third of the total increase at the end of the period. J D Ormond, before his purchase of Wallingford, grazed sheep 'on terms' or 'thirds' on the property of Richard Collins, a Wairarapa sheepfarmer. (1885,Balfour Diaries,75)

CRAWFORD Putting 'sheep on terms' was a very common custom in the early days. (1949,Sheep and Sheepmen of Canterbury,37)

on the blind¶

phr. (Rural) [of sheepdogs] out of sight of sheep being worked.

LILICO In training to run on the blind, open country without fences or landmarks of any kind should be chosen ... (1919,Breeding and Training of Sheep Dogs,19)

NEWTON The dog of course was 'running on the blind' ... (1964,Straggle Muster,16)

LOUGHNAN Blind, run on the: A dog is 'on the blind' when he is is running out of sight of his sheep. [running on the blind] (1981,Loughnan Glossary,7)

Championship to include running on the blind for not less than ten sheep in two separate lots. (Aug 1, 1919,NZ Farmer,986)

NEWTON It had been at least a two mile head run on the blind and for the greater part the little dog had been out of sight of his master. (1949,High Country Days,31)

MILLS ET AL Hold him [the sheepdog] until he does [sight the sheep] and he will then go away freely with the right direction and cast instead of running 'on the blind,' Running 'blind' often leads a dog into the bad habit of loitering out until he sights his sheep and is therefore to be avoided. (1964,Practical Guide to Handling Dogs and Stock,32)

LOUGHNAN A hard beat is usually one of the middle beats and very often includes bluffs and also much need to run dogs on the blind. (1981,Loughnan Glossary,6)

phr. without prior information or proper sight or foresight

LYON As the quartz mines began to decline in output and could no longer pay a large wage sheet, it became the custom to let them on tribute, by which parties of men were given the right to mine and raise quartz from the resultant crushing, of which a proportionate share of the gold went to the company. This system might also apply to old workings of mines in actual work, but in most of these cases the tributors followed a thin but valuable lead, which might not be much thicker than a finger, but so rich as to be worth tunnelling or working as a tribute, in which the golden lead was pursued on an upward slope. It used to be rather shrewdly suspected that tributors were not working on the blind' but had as former employees of the company marked a lead and covered the same by a piece of mine timber or rubble till a later date give them the chance to profit by their knowledge. Many of the tributors did exceedingly well; they had nothing except their labour to lose, and the company got some profit on a good lead, and sowhere's the harm?' (1952,Faring South,168)

SUTHERLAND ... the short staple of wool on the Merino allows a shearer to see and handle his work more easily. With the long wools, quite a lot of hand-work is done `on the blind'. (1953,Golden Bush, The,175,1963 ed.)

TUCKER [?horseracing] It was a question of backing on the blind because nobody was supposed to know in advance which mule carried which saddlecloth [indicating the number carried in the race]. (1978,Thoroughbreds Are My Life,26)

phr. (working) out of sight of the shepherd (of a sheepdog); often run on the blind

LILICO ... though he was very perfect on sheep within sight of him, he was no use at running on the blind in rough ground. (1934,Sheep Dog Memoirs,6)

HARTLEY Casting On the Blind</i>' ... A good opportunity is often presented when driving a mob through a paddock. A few will go off on a side track over a ridge. The trainer is certain of their whereabouts but it is a runon the blind' to the young dogs. . (1949,Shepherd's Dogs, The,25)

McLEOD On the blind: A dog running where he cannot see his sheep. (1972,Mountain World,310)

NEWTON RUNNING ON THE BLIND: A dog which is running out with no sheep in sight is `running on the blind.' (1949,High Country Days,197,1950 ed.)

NEWTON Running on the blind: When a dog is being sent after sheep which are out of his sight he is running on the blind. (1966,Boss's Story, The,189)

on the hoof¶

phr. (Rural) used with reference to live animals in contests, exhibitions, or for grading.

TAIT Every year there are one or more clients who are in the first four prizes, either 'on hoof' or 'on hooks.' (1956,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol I,20)

From a survey of lambs 'on the hoof' it appeared that the overall standard was very good ... (Jan 2, 1941,NZ Farmer,7)

Their winning entry in the lightweight national class was also placed second on the hoof by judges ... (Jun 1, 1994,NZ Farmer,26)

HINDMARSH .. there was mutton galore on the hoof ... (1997,Come Be a Pioneer,47)

1 phr. (Rural) [animals] walking rather than being transported.

NEWTON These [lambs] went to Fairfield on the hoof and killed out at the incredible average of over 42 pounds. (1973,Big Country of the South Island,239)

RICHARDS But by 1939 a change was beginning to take place in the movement of stock. No longer did all stock arrive on the hoof. (1989,Pioneer's Life, A,80)

They paid higher prices on the hoof than were justified. (Sep 1, 1925,NZ Farmer,1426)

In the early days all transport of stock was on the hoof, and it often took a runholder several weeks to reach his holding with his newly-acquired flocks. (May 15, 1945,NZ Journal of Agriculture,461)

MEEK The swagger was a pioneer of transport on the hoof. (1952,Station Days in Maoriland,24)

DAHM In those days all the stock had to be moved on the hoof. (2000,People of the Axe, The,46)

on the hooks¶

phr. (Rural) [butchery] used with reference to a slaughtered animal's final dressed carcass or its weight.

LOUGHNAN Hooks, on the: a term used in referring to the final dressed weight of a carcass. (1981,Loughnan Glossary,43)

I couldn't see any point in running a cattle operation where I paid more per kilogram liveweight than the equivalent of what I eventually got paid for them on the hooks. (Sep 1986,NZ Journal of Agriculture,08)

phr. (of slaughtered carcasses)

BOWEN Quick-maturing, well covered [145] with meat, and hanging up attractively `on the hooks', the Southdown has long been regarded as the premier meat breed of New Zealand. (1955,Wool Away!,144)

on the kivvy¶

phr. on the look out (qui vive)

WHITWORTH I was down there with Old Ashmore's lot, doing the trampoline, and so on; but things was very quizby. Everything went crooked somehow, and the gorger had a hawful cramp in his kick. Well, ramp was the word, and vamose we did, the lot on us; some here, some there. I was reg'lar beached, and I tell you when a cove 'as to spout his smish it's about time to look out. However, I got a job at a livery stable, but although that kep' me in grub, and I slep' in a spare stall, the billet was very multy, and I kep' on the kivvy for something better. [fn] 1. [trampoline] Vaulter. 2. [quizby] Bad. 3. [crooked] Wrong. 4. [cramp in his kick] Emptiness in his pockets. 5. [ramp] Clear out. 6. [vamose] Go. 7. [beached] Hard up. 8. [smish] Shirt. 9. [multy] No good. 10. [kivvy] Look out (qui vive.) (1879,Spangles and Sawdust,5)

on the wallaby¶

phr. on the swag (cf. wallaby track; wallaby trot) OED wallaby-track, and hence on the wallaby: Aust: wandering about on foot: 1869; AC 1861; Morris [orig. and usually Australian, and mainly station and outdoors usage in NZ]

BODELL He had the appearance of a fighting man or a dog fancier a brutish countenance and he had often been on the Wallaby (or tramp) about the various Gold Fields and Squatters Stations. [ed fn] A `dog fancier' was a receiver of stolen dogs who would return them to their owners for a fee. (1890,Soldier's View of Empire, A,141,1982 ed.)

McHUTCHESON He determined to see ... the interior of the country ..., and to that end he spent his first eighteen months or two years on the wallaby' ...humping his swag' in true Colonial fashion. (1892,Camp-Life in Fiordland,10 [?101])

WRIGHT (`Swagger') For them that's on the wallaby there's little to be had. (1897,Station Ballads,30)

BELL .. an' he'd be d-----d if he wad hump ony mair whusky to Swinborough on Shanks's powny. ... He micht juist as weel be on the wallaby amang the stations again - ... (1899,In the Shadow of the Bush,233)

Therefore, Woollcombe, get thee hence; go home thou wowser on the wallaby; hump bluey, carry Matilda, pad the hoof, thou parsonical howler, thou Wowseristic whaler Woollcombe. (Nov 6, 1909,NZ Truth,228/1/3)

phr. [cf P (B1942) 2. on an urban drinking-bout: Aust: ca1890-1910 3. penniless: Aust: 20c. the Red Funnel citation is probably mainly Australian in reference]

[From an articleOn the Wallaby' by Helen F. Hunt about wallaby-hunting in Australia being what gum-digging is in NZ] As the hunter was of necessity [?out] a good deal at night,on the wallaby' came by degrees to mean - according to the natural tendency of language to degenerate, when associated with low ideas - making a night of it', having aspree', being drunk; ... But to the rest of us on the wallaby' means taking off the harness, and letting ourselves run free a bit; putting aside the mask and coming down from the stage; loosening the grip of fashion and returning for a while to the simplicity of life ... We crave to go somewhereon the wallaby'. (1906,Red Funnel,3/147)

1 phr. (Rural) [used in Aus.] [itinerant worker] looking for work.

MARTIN These men were probably simply 'on the wallaby' in search of work. (1990,Forgotten Worker, The,29)

I never saw half as many men 'on the wallaby' at this time of year before. (Feb 21, 1880,Otago Witness,5)

ELKINGTON I wanted to go on the wallaby, as I had read so much about it. (1906,Adrift in NZ,24)

on thirds¶

n. a system of flock-sharing where an owner put his sheep into the care of a station owner who received a third of the wool and lambs in lieu of rent for grazing

1 phr. (Rural) a sheep-grazing arrangement where a grazier is allotted a proportion of the wool and offspring of the flock. [rural, usually sheep farming; occas thirds]

PAUL Many of our settlers have flocks of sheep placed out 'on thirds' as it is called ... (1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,21)

Mr. Brook will be happy to take (Cattle) on Thirds, or other terms. (Mar 31, 1843,New Zealand Colonist & Port Nicholson Advertiser,1)

WANTED to Let 1400 acres, on Castle Rock Run. Terms liberal - small quantity of stock (sheep if preferred), will be given on thirds. (Feb 11, 1867,Southland Times,1)

Reginald Alexander, farmer and stock dealer, Hikutaia, said he was farming on thirds with his father and a soldier brother (returned). (Feb 21, 1917,Ohinemuri Gazette,2)

Is sharemilker on thirds entitled to his proportion on the gross or on the net butter-fat profit? (Jan 6, 1923,NZ Truth,12)

Stafford renewed his offer to me of looking after his sheep on thirds, ... (Aug 23, 1849,Torlesse Papers,99,1958 ed.)

GODLEY It is customary here, & sometimes very profitable, to have sheep 'on thirds'; which means to send them to the station, & allow the owner of it half the wool, & 1/3d. of the increase for the trouble & expense of looking after them - the owner receives half the wool, & 2/3ds. of the increase, as profit on his capital. (Jul 8, 1850,Letter) [NOTES: 1850 July 8 Godley letter in 1971 Speeches and Documents 28]

ROCHFORT A settler will generally take sheep on thirds; that is, he takes the sheep on his run, and attends to them, finding labour: the party to whom the sheep belong gives him annually one-third of the increase and half the wool, besides paying for material for dressing in case of scab, &c. (1853,Adventures of a Surveyor,27)

ADAMS or place his sheep `on thirds', as it is called; that is to say, may enter into an arrangement with the owner of some large sheep farm, to receive a certain proportion of the wool and of the increase, in lieu of rent. (1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,68) [NOTES: (Griffiths Collection)]

... sheep on thirds (1854,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,21) [NOTES: 1854 Paul Canterbury Settlement 21]

HURSTHOUSE His best plan is to get acquainted with some practical flock-master in the neighbourhood, and to make the arrangement of `thirds'. (1857,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/398)

JOLLIE Watt, who was starting a sheep-farm in the Wairau Valley [before 1850 was] on `Thirds' for three years. This meant one third of the wool and lambs each year to Watt and I have two thirds. (1878,Reminiscences,17)

Thirds.' </i>- All endowment lands leased under the [Land] Act are subject to thethirds' clause, which gives one-third of the rent to the local bodies for roads for access. (1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,188)

(1933,Press, The) [NOTES: Acland [check???]]

AYSON Mr. Shaw took on what was called thirds at lamb marking time, and put his own earmark on every third lamb [of Brugh's flock running with his]. (1937,Pioneering in Otago,94)

BEATTIE Johnny Jones saw that Trotter was a reliable man and gave him a start [in the 1850s] with a flock of 1000 sheep on thirds - that is to say, Jones got the wool and two-thirds of the lambs while Trotter got one-third for his work. (1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,100)

ADAMS .. and as his means may permit, either rent and stock a large run, or place his sheep 'on thirds' as it is called. (1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A,68)

Mr Fannin is prepared to receive sheep on thirds on his run, Rua Taniwha plains, Ahuriri. (Feb 20, 1858,Hawke's Bay Herald,1)

Wanted: Sheep on thirds. Apply to WR Ferguson, Clive. (May 27, 1859,Hawke's Bay Herald,1)

BALFOUR Under this arrangement, a landless man, who had enough capital to buy sheep, could graze them on another man's property. Usually the grazier took one-half of the annual wool clip of the 'terms' sheep and one-third of the total increase at the end of the period. J D Ormond before his purchase of Wallingford, grazed sheep 'on terms' or 'thirds' on the property of Richard Collins, a Wairarapa sheepfarmer. (1885,Balfour Diaries,75)

on-cue feeding¶

n. alternative term for demand feeding

Another term currently hot on the baby jargon scene is "on-cue feeding." Whereas demand feeding ran along the lines of feeding the baby up to 94 times a day (but hey, who's counting?), on cue feeding runs along the lines of feeding the baby up to 94 times a day. but in a much more co-operative cued-on sort of way. (Jul 29, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),38)

on-off grazing¶

1 n. (Rural) a form of rotational grazing [where stock are offered pasture or crop for only three to four hours each day and are fed on a wintering-pad or sacrifice paddock for the other twenty hours.]

Using humps and hollows, on-off grazing, being attuned to the conditions and seeing problems before you get to them are the ways the O'Briens cope with the wet. (Sep 4, 1997,NZ Farmer,24)

If you find that you cannot get density in your sward .. you are thrown back to on-off grazing systems... (Aug 1972,NZ Dairy Exporter,9)

WHITE & HODGSON On/off grazing is where animals are offered their daily pasture allowance (three to four hours' grazing) and then removed from the pasture to a hard stand-off area, wintering pad or sacrifice area. (1999,NZ Pasture and Crop Science,156)

on/off the hill¶

phr. the hill, the high-country mustering beat; ?in gen. the high country itself; be on the hill, to be out mustering; be off the hill, to be finished mustering. [(musterers')]

No shepherd or musterer speaks of mountains. He may be mustering the side of Mount Cook, but he is, still on the h. (C.) (Oct 28, 1933,Press, The,17)

I was two beats from the top, and there was shingle all the way. It slid continuously, and all the time I had to bear up hill, to keep a level track. once a day¶

1 phr. (Rural) [dairying] milking cows once daily before drying off or to restrict milking to once daily for animal husbandry reasons.

Traditionally the shift to once a day is generally used as a signal to the herd that drying off time is not too far off. The expectation is that once a day will initiate a reduction in milk production. However, there is also the obvious positive side to once a day. Once a day will increase cow condition (or decrease condition loss.) (Apr 4, 2001,Fencepost.com,Expert Farming)

Cow condition is no longer a concern under his once-a-day milking regime. When we went to once-a-day milking they very quickly picked up condition. (Jul 26, 2002,Dominion,C7)

Mrs Tong said that because the once-a-day approach required a slightly bigger herd, it did take longer to milk ...Our once-a-day cows were at least half a condition score above the twice-a-day cows throughout the year, so they are heavier. [once-a-day] (Sep 20, 2002,Taranaki Daily News,Farming Supplement 5)

one-way¶

adj. [Jim writes : Dictionaries define one-way in relation to traffic as allowing travel in one direction only. This does not cover the common NZ one-way bridge, across which a vehicle may travel in either direction unless there is opposing traffic. That is, it can be crossed only one way at a time. Although the distinction is a fine one, it seems real. Possibly the term one-way bridge is itself distinctive NZE. The latest NZ Road Code uses the term one-lane bridge.]

opalfish¶

n.

Hemerocoetes acanthorhynchus (Forster). Opal-fish. (1938,TrRSNZ,68/415) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

GRAHAM OPAL-FISH Hemerocoetes monopterygius (Bloch and Schneider) ... Both anglers and commercial fishermen of Port Chalmers knew this fish and had bestowed the common name ofOpal-fish prior to my having seen one. The opalescent display of colours left no doubt in my mind as to the appropriateness of the common name. The abovementioned anglers and fishermen had various names to distinguish this fish from Opal-fish - which are as follows: Bluey, Blue-cap, Blue-Bonnet, which I have used. (1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,316,2nd ed.)

GRAHAM Opal Fish Hemerocoetes monopterygius (Bloch and Schn.) (Syn. Callionymus acanthorhynchus Bloch and Schn., Hemerocoetes microps Waite) (1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,410) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

PAUL Opalfishes Family Percophidae. There are several New Zealand species, one of which is common in harbours and over the shelf. The opalfish, Hemerocoetes monopterygius, is so named from its irridescent striped and banded colouring of blue, orange... (1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,116)

Open Bay Islands gecko¶

n. a small brown gecko known only from Taumaka Island in the Open Bay Islands, south Westland. [Hoplodactylus "Open Bay Islands gecko"]

Other notable faunainclude the endemic Open Bay Islands leech and Open Bay Islands skink (Oligosoma "Open Bay Islands"), Open Bay Islands gecko (Hoplodactylus "Open Bay Islands"), and large numbers of fur seals (Arctocephalus forsteri). (Sep 1997,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.doc.govt.nz/upload/documents/science-and-technical/Sfc057.pdf]

Taumaka is the only known location for three critically endangered species: the Open Bay Islands leech, the Open Bay Islands gecko, and the Open Bay Islands skink . . . (Apr 2007,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.westmarine.org.nz/documents/WCMarineandCoastalEnvironment.pdf]

Open Bay Islands skink¶

n. Known only from a small population on Taumaka Island in the Open Bay Islands, South Westland the Open Bay Islands skink is slender, dark brown, with relatively large feet. [Oligasoma taumakae aka Taumaka skink.]

Tony Whitaker and John Lyall An undescribed taxon with unresolved relationships. .. This clearly requires furtherinvestigation as it would be hard to find two more disparate species withinOligosoma than the Open Bay Islands skink and the scree skink.not just inform, but also in habitat and behaviour. (May 2004,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://doc.govt.nz/upload/documents/science-and-technical/WClizards.pdf Conservation of lizards in WestCoast/Tai Poutini ConservancyTony Whitaker and John LyallPub by DOC Wellington May 2004]

Miller, Daugherty The allozyme data revealed the existence of three undescribed taxa, whichwe have labelled: O. "Big Bay", O. " Grey Valley" and O . " Open Bay Islands". O."Open Bay Islands" remains undescribed due to a lack of collected materialfrom this species. The other two species are being formally described in apaper to be submitted to the New Zealand Journal of Zoology. (2000,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.conservation.gen.nz/upload/documents/science-and-technical/casn291.pdf Identification of genetic diversity Conservation Advisory Science Notes No. 291 ,Department of Conservation, Wellington.for lizard conservationMiller, C.; Daugherty, C.H., 2000.]

open shed¶

n. [(shearers') check SHED (v. contract shearing)]

BARKER Brown and Wetherby's was an `open shed', where any shearers that came were taken on until there were hands enough. (1872,Xmas Cake in Four Quarters, A,Ch. III)

Open shed.-S. where the shearers are not engaged beforehand, but roll up on the advertised day and take their chance of employment. I think the custom has quite gone out of use now. Anama in the late 'nineties was the last station I can remember to have seen advertising its starting day. (Jan 27, 1934,Press, The,15)

ACLAND Clayton was an `open shed', that is, the shearers were not engaged beforehand, but turned up and took their chance of a pen on the advertised starting day. (1946,Early Canterbury Runs,166)

1 n. (Rural) a woolshed where shearers arrive and compete for a stand without prior reservation.

VANCE Sometimes shearers had to ford flooded rivers so as to be at an 'open shed' station on time. 'Open shed' stations picked their shearers from those present when the shed opened; there were no prior engagements and no jobs for latecomers. (1965,High Endeavour,51)

LOUGHNAN open shed: as distinct from a closed shed, this was one in which shearers used to be chosen from amongst those who were there in time when the annual shearing began. (1981,Loughnan Glossary,II 2)

Shearing will commence at Benmore Station, on Friday, 11th November. An Open Shed. (Oct 28, 1876,North Otago Times,3)
