MARTIN C G Tripp's Orari Gorge station in South Canterbury ... was a well-known open shed. (1990,Forgotten Worker, The,37)

open slather¶

n. (informal) a free-for-all, a free rein; anything goes [(ca1920 p.c. R. Mason (1952)) compare DNZE earliest citation 1959]

However, Wellington Central MP and ACT New Zealand justice spokesperson Stephen Franks says in practice council's [sic]will have to pass special new by-laws, and until they do it is "open slather". (Jul 1, 2003,Independent Herald (Wellington),1)

It was our fate to witness the misfortune of the sinking of the Triumph near by, and when next morning the Majestic followed it made us want still more to 'Up and at them'. However, things will not and cannot be always in our favor, but it [is] hard to grin and bear it at times, especially when your side loses without a fair go, or what we call 'an open slather'. (Jul 15, 1915,Poverty Bay Herald,8) [NOTES: from a letter from Gunner Pat Duncan at Gallipoli to a friend in Gisborne]

Llewellyn, S.P. The petrol was discovered about six miles down the Amyntaion-Servia road and as soon as the lorries were loaded our drivers turned their attention to the ration section of the dump. They were shown where the luxuries were stacked and told to help themselves. It was their first experience of what was to be known later as an 'open slather' and many of them were pink with excitement as they scrambled among the stacks, breaking open cases of tinned fruit, tinned vegetables, pickles, jam, tobacco, cigarettes. You just helped yourself. It was a childhood dream come true and for a quarter of an hour they behaved like greedy small boys. They stuffed their blouses with tins and threw cases on top of the loads of petrol.Often, in the days that lay ahead, we were to be wet and cold, sick and sorry, tired and frightened-never hungry. Empty and half-empty tins of peas and beans and pears and raspberries were to mark the passage of our convoys. It is probably fair to say that from the day of the first 'open slather' until the day we left Greece most of us over-ate. (1949,Journey Towards Christmas,51)

It is open season all the year round for the shooting of bank clerks. Even the wild ducks get a sanctuary sometimes, but with the underpaid and responsibility-laden banker it is an open slather all the year round. (Dec 6, 1924,NZ Truth,5) [NOTES: Truth's 'Melbourne Rep.' is reporting on a spate of bank shootings there]

The last round was a 'trimmer'. Both boys let themselves go, and made it an open slather. The crowd was on its feet throughout. (Jan 3, 1929,NZ Truth,8)

If the poachers know all they have to be is staunch ... then it's open slather. (Feb 13, 2004,Dominion Post,A3)

I think his [a boxer named Torrie's] will be one of the best professional fights the Association has ever put on and it will be open slather from the gong. I am sure this event will be worth the price of admission. (Mar 26, 1918,Grey River Argus,4)

v.

This industry gives employment to a large number of persons, both directly to those engaged in breeding, shearing, freezing, and shipping, and indirectly to those who labour at bushfelling, grass-seed sowing, fencing, and otherwise opening up new country. (1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,11)

open winter¶

n. [CC Ransom notes: But open winter is an old American term for a mild winter: we always thought of freedom from heavy snow as its chief characteristic: thus countryside is open for travel, movement of beasts, etc]

NEDWTON ~: In winter snow forms a boundary for stock. If the winter is mild this boundary may not be sufficient; the country is then open for sheep to get away back into dangerous areas - hence the term `open winter.' (1949,High Country Days,196,1950 ed.) [NOTES: Gloss.]

1 n. (Rural) [high country] a relatively snow-free season that does not allow a clear boundary for safe sheep grazing.

Italian ryegrass has seldom been destroyed by frost, but it runs the risk of being rotted out by over luxuriance in an open winter. (Apr 15, 1862,Wellington Independent,3)

We had a very mild and open winter. (Aug 16, 1873,Otago Witness,10)

NEWTON Open winter: In winter snow forms a boundary for stock. If the winter is mild, this boundary may not be sufficient; the country is then open for sheep to get away back into dangerous areas - hence the term 'open winter.' (1949,High Country Days,196)

After an unusually long period of fine open winter weather - more like summer indeed - the rain, which has been threatening the last day or two, is now coming down in earnest - most acceptably for the farmers, who were feeling the want of seasonable moisture. (Sep 3, 1859,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,2)

We are still enjoying a fine open winter, and farmers are taking advantage of the beautiful weather by pushing forward with sowing. (Jul 25, 1889,Otago Witness,17)

We have had a very open winter, having had more frost than the majority of us have seen here for some years past. (Aug 9, 1894,Otago Witness,23)

Farm work in the district is well forward, owing to the open winter we are having, and so far everything points to an early seed time. (Jul 18, 1904,Southland Times,3)

There is also a drop of 1/4d to 1/2d in frozen meat quotations just now, which does not tend to make the sheep market any livelier, also notwithstanding the very open winter we have experienced hitherto, there is an impression abroad that the turnip crops will hardly see the winter out. (Aug 15, 1911,Otautau Standard & Wallace County Chronicle,4)

The open winter has certainly been responsible for a much-improved range of values for July than was anticipated, and with that month behind farmers have lost all fear of a shortage of feed. (Jul 27, 1920,Otautau Standard & Wallace County Chronicle,3)

The exceptionally open winter we have had has, by bringing forward the grass, greatly increased our town meltings. (May 18, 1872,Otago Witness,4)

Operation Deepfreeze¶

n. US Antarctic programme

In the early '60s the streets of Dunedin were filled with American sailors. They were part of Operation Deepfreeze, transporting supplies and personnel across the world's widest ocean to Scott Base and McMurdo. (Nov 7, 2004,Sunday Star Times,C6)

Operation Frostbite¶

1 n. (Rural) the milking of dairy cows through winter.

It is known in Canterbury as Operation Frostbite - factory suppliers who have chosen to brave the frost and cold to continue milking through the depths of winter. (Jul 5, 2001,Taranaki Daily News,17)

operations manager¶

1 n. (Rural) a management position on a large dairy farm.

Operations manager 1000 cows, Patoka. Our clients are seeking an Operations manager who is capable of implementing budgets and taking responsibility for their 400 ha (effective) operation in the Hawkes Bay. (Mar 3, 2001,Dominion,54)

Captain Ahab had Moby Dick, the great white whale, in the 19th century novel about truth, nature and God. Last-century New Zealanders had Opo the dolphin, who for one magic summer persuaded people of the benevolence in nature. (Jul 29, 2001,Sunday Star Times,A7)

Opoitian¶

adj. rock classification

Barry McInnis, now working as assistant micropalaeontologist at B.P., Gisborne, is to determine the microfaunas of the type Opoitian for an M.Sc. thesis. (Jun 3, 1961,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,10/2)

In most areas sheets separated by thick mudstone and sandstone units occur in Opoitian, Waipipian and Mangapanian rocks. (Nov 1978,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,46/3)

orange blossom¶

n. Hoheria populnea (See lacebark)

FEATON HOHERIA POPULNEA (A.Cunn.) ... The HOUI' orWHAU-WHI'.- ... grows abundantly in both Islands. ... Owing to the chaste [snow-white] appearance of its flowers, it has not inaptly been termed by some the New Zealand orange Blossom'. The inner bark is peculiar, and consists of layer upon layer of laced fibre, ...From this peculiarity it is called by the settlers theThousand Jacket', lace-bark', orRibbon-wood'. (1889,Art Album NZ Flora,55)

JOHNSON In November 1975 [the Japanese research vessel Shinkai Maru] was trawling on the Chatham Rise when she brought up a full bag of brightly orange-coloured fish. MAF scientist Gavin James and his Japanese counterpart Tadashi Inada had never seen the fish before, nor could they find it in their identification guides. After a careful inspection they decided it looked a bit like an inshore fish of the roughy family. It was orange, so they recorded it as 'orange roughy'. (2004,Hooked,284)

The fish, known as the `rattail' or Coelinchorus, were caught off the coast of ... Taranaki, in the orange roughy grounds at a depth of 800m ... The rattail also known as the grenadier is closely related to the codfish family, feeding on the bottom of the ocean ... Of the 40 known species of Coelinchorus 11 of them seem to be indigenous only to New Zealand says Mr Paulins. Notes check spelling Coelinchorus (Jul 22, 1988,Evening Post,14)

Orange roughy: kiwifruit of the sea, the fish which is a blueprint for the export market, with its firm-textured, white, odourless fillets. Or, to be technical, hoplostethus atlanticus: average length, 30 to 40cm; average weight 1.5kg. Orange body and fins, deep body, massive head with conspicuous bony ridges and cavities. A deep water species, irregular in distribution and abundance. Caught by trawling, main season May to August, resource size unknown butundoubtedly large. (Aug 1, 1983,National Business Review,33)

orange-fronted parakeet¶

n. the parakeet Cyanoramphus malherbi [Also known as the South Island parakeet and orange crested parakeet, the endangered orange-fronted parakeet could be forgiven for having an identity crisis, since the debate over its taxonomic status has spanned three centuries.]

The orange-fronted parakeet has a pale lemon-yellow crown, an orange forehead band and orange rump spots, while the yellow-crowned has a bright golden-yellow crown, with crimson frontal band and rump spots. (Aug 2001,NZ Geographic,No. 52/14)

Orange-fronted parakeet Cyanoramphus malherbi (SOUANCE', 1857) ... Since European settlement the orange-fronted parakeet has never been as common as other New Zealand parakeets. ... OTHER NAME: Alpine parakeet. ... Probably now in the South Island only. (1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,247)

The first recorded mention of the orange-fronted parakeet was in 1857 by Charles De Souance, who named it Cyanoramphus malherbi from a single specimen, source unknown. (May 2002,NZ Geographic,51/10)

orange-leaf¶

n. Coprosma lucida ORANGE-LEAF (TREE)

HOOKER Orange-leaf of Otago. Coprosma lucida, etc. (1867,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),766)

LINDSAY [Coprosma lucida, Forst.] The Broad-leaf' orOrange-leaf' tree of the settler; terms, however, which are probably not only applied to other species of the genus Coprosma, (e.g. C. robusta, Raoul,) but to species of genera belonging to very different families - and especially to Griselinia lucida, (N.O. Corneae,) whose foliage closely resembles that of Coprosma lucida ... C. lucida is also known to the Otago natives as Kalamou' (Hector); and to the North Island Maoris asKaramu', [fn] Kakaramu,' orKarangu,' [fn] (Colenso.) [fn] Also applied to C. foetidissima. (1868,Contributions to NZ Botany,71)

MORRIS Orange-Tree, n. The New Zealand Orange-Tree is a name given to the Tarata (q.v.), from the aromatic odour of its leaves when crushed. (1898,Austral English,332)

Concealed by the shadow of a large orange-tree, they passed her unobserved... (Nov 5, 1877,North Otago Times,5)

Bloom of the orange-tree / Right well becometh thee... (Apr 3, 1873,Tuapeka Times,8)

orange-wattled crow¶

n. (See also crow)

The orange-wattled crow is sometimes mistaken for [the huia] by trampers. (Sep 4, 1944,NZEF Times,IV/167 : 10)

The orange-wattled crow.. very much resembles the North Island species with the exception of the wattles, which are red or reddish-brown. (Apr 17, 1890,Otago Witness,18)

G. cinerea. - Kokako, Orange-wattled Crow. South Island. Very irregular in distribution. (1886,TrNZI,18/112) [NOTES: (Martin)]

Sir James Hector, in drawing attention.. to a very fine specimen of the New Zealand crow or jay. as it should more properly be called, made interesting references to the complete isolation of the orange wattle crow, in the South Island, and the blue wattle crow in the North Island. (Dec 21, 1897,Otago Witness,10)

n. (Environment) (See kokako)

HUTTON 41. GLAUCOPIS CINEREA. Gml. Orange-wattled Crow. Callaeas Wilsoni. BULLER. (1871,Birds of New Zealand,16)

BULLER GLAUCOPIS CINEREA. (ORANGE-WATTLED CROW.) ... Native name.- Kokako. ... This species is the South-Island representative of Glaucopis wilsoni, to which it bears a general resemblance, except in the colour of its wattles and its rather smaller size. (1873,Birds of New Zealand,155,1888 2nd edn I/5 ed.)

G. cinerea. - Kokako, Orange-wattled Crow. South Island. Very irregular in distribution. (1886,TrNZI,18/112) [NOTES: (Martin)]

... crows [are represented] by a genus - Glaucopsis - ... with one species in the north and another in the south (the orange-wattled crow). (1900,Canterbury Old & New,195)

Callaeas cinerea Gm. Orange-wattled Crow. With the spread of settlement the diminution of the numbers of this tame species was almost inevitable. (1923,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,6/97) [NOTES: Myers Endemic Birds]

HOWARD [Stewart Island birds include] the fern-bird (Bowdleria fulva), the bell-bird ... and in the second class: Orange-wattled crow (Glaucopis cinerea) ... (1940,Rakiura,323)

POWELL BLUE-WATTLED CROW (Callaeas wilsoni). A moderately large dark bluish grey bird with a stout black bill, ... and conspicuous wattles of bright blue. The South Island species, C. cinerea, is almost identical except for the wattles, which are orange....The Orange-wattled crow is still plentiful...The Maori name for both species is Kokako. (1947,Native Animals of NZ,87)

Kokako Callaeas cinerea (GMELIN, 1788) ... North Island Kokako C.c. wilsoni (BONAPARTE, 1851). South Island Kokako C.c. cinerea (GMELIN, 1788). OTHER NAMES: Blue wattled crow, blue gill, orange wattled crow, organ bird. (1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,303)

orange-wattled kokako¶

2 n. (Environment)

Glaucopis wilsoni. - Kokako, Blue-wattled Crow. North Island. Very irregular in distribution. (1886,TrNZI,18/112) [NOTES: (Martin)]

McLINTOCK There are two races of kokako, Callaeas cinerea wilsoni, the blue-wattled kokako, which occurs in the North Island, and Callaeas cinerea cinerea, the orange-wattled kokako, inhabiting the South Island and Stewart Island. (1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,2/235)

CROWE Potato orchid Gastrodia cunninghami Huperi... The thick, starchy, tuberous roots formed a part of the winter food supply of the maori, especially of Tuhoe of the Urewera district. When being sought by the Tuhoe the tubers would be called maukuku, not huperei; if the wrong name was mentioned during the search, the roots were said to hide themselves. (1997,Field Guide to Native Edible Plants of NZ,57)

ordinary claim¶

3 n.

It will be necessary not only to grant prospecting claims, but to define what shall be the size of ordinary claims; to make provision for water-bailing, for the amalgamation of claims, and for the issue of leases, so as to encourage the erection of proper machinery for pumping and crushing. (Feb 1, 1862,Otago Witness,5)

At this Court yesterday, Warden McCarthy granted the following applications .. A. Otway for mail tail race and ordinary claims. (Jul 7, 1904,Southland Times,3)

Growcott and Hartke (Mr Wells), surrender of ordinary claim at Seddon's Terrace. - Accepted. (Jun 6, 1919,Grey River Argus,4)

If neither of them should get anything payable then the prospectors may be said to have all the gold in their own claim, which would be nothing new in this country, where the leads are so short, and in this instance where the prospecting claim is so long, being equivalent to two ordinary claims, so that it is nothing wonderful if some of the many claims marked out on either side of them should be duffers. (May 5, 1876,Grey River Argus,2)

At this Court yesterday, Warden McCarthy granted the following applications .. A. Otway for mail tail race and ordinary claims. (Jul 7, 1904,Southland Times,3)

An ordinary claim must not exceed an area of five acres. (Jul 3, 1930,NZ Truth,24)

WEKEY Claims shall be classified thus;- 1. Ordinary Claims, i.e., alluvial claims worked without the aid of sluices or machines. 2. Sluice and machine claims, i.e., alluvial claims worked with the aid of sluices or puddling machines. 3. Wet Claims, i.e., alluvial claims in flooded ground ... 4. Creek or river claims, i.e., alluvial claims in the bed of creeks or rivers. 5. Quartz claims. (1862,Otago As It Is,61) [NOTES: (quoting Otago Gfds Regs)]

Phil. T. Green, application for protection for ordinary claim at the Waiho river, for three months - Granted. (Jul 30, 1895,West Coast Times,4)

Three miners, of whom I am one, applied for an ordinary claim. We proved it to be payable, but because a man named Buchanan squatted on it as the owner of residence area, the Warden refused to grant our application. (Aug 11, 1888,West Coast Times,4)

Claims are classified under five heads, vis.: Ordinary claims, i.e., alluvial claims, worked without the aid of sluices or machines.. The size of Claims is then fixed as follows: Ordinary Claims shall not exceed 30 feet by 30 feet for each person, unless the depth of sinking exceeds 50 feet, in which case such claim may be extended to 35 feet by 35 feet. (Jul 5, 1862,Otago Witness,6)

ore to ore¶

phr. [(goldmining)]

The ore-shoots of all the approximately parallel Hauraki veins occurred in corresponding positions in the veins, and pitched in the same direction as the main shoot. This is the structure termed in the older mining phraseology ore to ore', or shootsback to back'. (1907,NZ Geol. Survey Bulletin,No. 4 (N.S.): 106) [NOTES: (Fraser and Adams Coromandel)]

I have chosen the Maori term `Orea' to apply to this eel [Anguilla dieffenbachii Gray], by which name it is universally called in North Auckland. (1932,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,13/228) [NOTES: Phillipps]

oreshoot¶

n.

McARA Oreshoot: The payable zone within a reef. In some formations oreshoots follow a kind of pattern but in the quartz fissure veins of the Waihi district they occur sporadically. They generally became shorter and smaller at depth. (1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),328)

Oretian¶

adj. Geol. classification

Foraminifera found in Oretian and Warepan rocks in North Island constitute an important addition to New Zealand's Triassic foraminiferal fauna, already by far the largest known in the Southern Hemisphere. (Jul 1984,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,65/19)

organ bird¶

n. (Environment) the indigenous bird, kokako [ORIGIN: Maori]

NICHOLAS There was however one bird that was distinguished from all the rest, as well by the compass and variety of its notes, as by their incomparable sweetness. This bird, which has been brought to Port Jackson, and highly prized there, is called by the colonists [335] the organ bird, and is, I believe, peculiar to New Zealand: ... (1817,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1/334)

SHEPHERD This day returned to Albion cove saw ... a Parot of brownish colour and a large white bird as large as a turky and a small sort of brown teal duck, a large black and white Duck called a Paradise Duck a black bird called the organ bird shags a Black Robin with a white breast ... (Mar 9, 1826,Journal) [NOTES: 1826 Mar 9 Shepherd Journal in 1940 Howard Rakiura 359]

The organ-bird', says Mr. Nicholas,is, I believe, peculiar to New Zealand. The notes of thenightingale .... are, in my opinion, much inferior to the song of this bird.' (1835,Some Particulars Concerning NZ,12)

FALLA et al. KOKAKO Callaeas cinerea Other names: Wattled Crow, Organ-bird, Gill-bird, Bluegill (N.I.). (1966,Birds of New Zealand,236)

Kokako Callaeas cinerea (GMELIN, 1788) ... North Island Kokako C.c. wilsoni (BONAPARTE, 1851). South Island Kokako C.c. cinerea (GMELIN, 1788). OTHER NAMES: Blue wattled crow, blue gill, orange wattled crow, organ bird. (1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,303)

Kokako ( Callaeas cinerea wilsoni ) sometimes known as the blue-wattled crow, or the organ bird, is one of the country's most threatened species. At most only about 3000 survive. (May 16, 2009,Your Weekend,13)

Orion¶

n. the Shacklock Orion range

to / taste the fine Scots shortbread fired in the coal range / heat. "SHACKLOCK ORION" the iron door stated... (Sep 28, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),61) [NOTES: Jeffrey Paparoa Holman]

BURTON In 1873, however, an enterprising English settler by the name of Henry Shacklock produced the prototype of a new coal range, which he later called the Orion. Here at last was an oven designed for New Zealand conditions; its fire box was wide and shallow to draw in the extra air needed for lignite coal, and it did not need to be bricked in. The No. 1 Orion soon outsold all its competitors, ..., and was built substantially the same way until the 1930s. (1982,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,23)

...he was also chief fireman in the run up to Christmas, and that meant keeping up a constant supply of firewood for the black-leaded Orion range that squatted in a corner of the kitchen...[52] The Christmas cake would have been made a couple of weeks earlier, but there were trays of cakes to be baked in the oven of the old Orion stove, as Mum and GRandma and Aunt Liz hustled around and generally got in each other's way. (Dec 2002,NZ Memories,No. 39/51)

Orioris were also used to teach children life skills such as growing and gathering food. (Aug 11, 1986,Dominion)

Otago cockney¶

n.

THOMSON ...it is worthy of notice, that the colonists, soon after the formation of all the settlements, acquired distinguishing epithets; thus there was an Auckland cove, a Wellington swell, a Nelson snob, a Taranaki exquisite, an Otago cockney, and a Canterbury pilgrim. These epithets, almost already forgotten, are too characteristic to be buried in oblivion, as each originated in some peculiarity of the leading colonists. The Otago settlers derived theirs from their ignorance of the country in which they lived; the Auckland people from their extensive Sydney connexions; and the origin of the other names are sufficiently obvious without explanation. (1859,Story of New Zealand, The,2/188)

McGILL Otago cockney nickname for person from Otago province. (1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,79)

Otago ghost moth¶

n.

Larvae of the Otago ghost moth (Aoraia rufivena) spend their days underground but emerge at night to feed on leaf litter, including that of snow tussock. (Nov 2004,NZ Geographic,No. 66/43)

Otago plesiosaur¶

n.

The Otago plesiosaur, named Kaiwhekea katiki, "squid-eater of Katiki," is the only example of its species ever found, and the largest fossil yet recovered in New Zealand. (Nov 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 66/15)

SINCLAIR ... in 1859, ... , there occurred an incident known as the Otago Raid'. The wily Scots, hearing of unemployment in the capital, carried off a hundred settlersfrom the mild, temperate and fruitful North, to the frigid, bleak and snowy South, ... (1959,History of New Zealand,104)

Otago schist¶

n. Geol. definition

It is perhaps especially notable in that it completes Mr Wood's reconnaissance mapping of the Fiordland Complex and his mapping of the Otago schists. (Jul 1967,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,23/8)

Furthermore, some of the colour matches and matching of screens between sheets are not good, especially in the case of the metamorphic subzones of the Otago schists, the Pareora series, certain Permian units and the Pomona granite. (Jul 1967,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,23/9)

Professor Hutton's work in New Zealand was on two main topics, the study of progressive metamorphism in the Otago schists, particularly in the Lake Wakatipu region, and work on heavy minerals in the gravels and sands of the west coast of the South Island. (Mar 1972,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,32/13)

Full Day Excursions: F.7, Middlemarch-Macraes. Otago schist, scheelite deposits, landforms. (May 1969,Geological Society of NZ Newsletter,27/2)

Otago skink¶

n. (Environment) a splendid and highly endangered skink with brightly mottled appearance, relatively widespread in Otago and capable of living up to 40 years. [Oligosoma otagense]

The critically endangered Otago skink is the South Island's largest skink, and is found only amongst rocky outcrops in tussock grassland. (2006,Internet - see Notes,2) [NOTES: www.doc.govt.nz/upload/documents/about-doc/concessions-and-permits/conservation .../central-otago-plants-animals-lowres.pdf.]

The Otago skink, the largest reptile on the New Zealand mainland, grows up to 300 mm long and lives on schist outcrops, hunting for insects in the surrounding tussock. (Nov 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 66/34)

The Otago skink and grand skink are unique to Central Otago and are the largest skinks in New Zealand. Grand skinks are black with yellowish flecks, while Otago skinks are black with grey, green, or yellowish blotches. (2006,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.conservation.govt.nz/upload/documents/about-doc/concessions-and-permits/conservation-revealed/skinks-geckos-lowres.pdf]

A mammal-proof fence around an 18-ha enclosure was built by the Department of Conservation near Macraes Flat in eastern North Otago, to protect remnant populations of the critically endangered grand skink (Oligosoma grande) and Otago skink (Oligosoma otagense) (Reardon 2006). (Dec 3, 2007,Internet - see Notes,169) [NOTES: http://www.newzealandecology.org/nzje/]

Jewell Otago skink Oligosoma otagense .. One of our most spectacular and most endangered lizard species. (2008,Reptiles and Amphibians of New Zealand,75)

The largest reptile on the mainland today lives in the schist outcrops of Otago's tussock grasslands. In this classic lizard country - dry, warm and rocky - Otago skinks soak up the sun's heat on the many convenient ledges, retreating to nearby cracks and crevices if disturbed. (Jul 2000,NZ Geographic,No. 47/64)

Otago skinks and the equally rare grand skink, which also inhabits the rocky outcrops of Otago's tussock grassland, are hardly ever found in areas that have been intensively farmed. (Jul 2000,NZ Geographic,No. 47/65)

At Macraes Flat, an hour's drive [47] north-west of Dunedin, Tocher and other DoC staff are struggling to maintain the last sizable populations of grand and Otago skinks. (Nov 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 66/46)

Jenny Jones The other large skinks in New Zealand are the Otago skink and the chevron skink. (2002,Lizards,31)

Four captive-bred Otago skinks from Wellington were officially welcomed by the Central Otago Ecological Trust(COET), in partnership with the Department of Conservation and Landcare Research. (Nov 3, 2005,Internet - see Notes) [NOTES: http://www.landcareresearch.co.nz/news/release.asp?Ne_ID=185]

James Reardon Two of the largest, most colourful species of skink - the grand and the Otago - can easily be identified thanks to their bold, almost mesmerising livery. Shiny black with bold blotches of gold, the Otago skink (Oligosoma otagense) can reach 30 cm in length and is a true giant among the country's endemic skinks. (Jan 2006,NZ Geographic,77/93)

"You need something attractive," he says. "And these Otago skinks are big, they're black with gold spots all over them, and they're spectacular." (Sep 2009,NZ Geographic,99/18)

When the Great Otago Skink Recovery Programme began at Macraes Flat in 2004, the populations of Otago and grand skinks - New Zealand's largest and rarest lizards - were so low it was thought both species would be functionally extinct within the decade. (Sep 2009,NZ Geographic,99/18)

Otaian¶

The DSIR's latest lucerne, Otaio, has already been a decade in the research pipeline. After 10 years of trials, the crop research division now has 2 kg of Otaio seed. ibid. 22 Otaio has proved itself in Canterbury field trials to be resistant to the aphids and other diseases.. (Nov 1986,NZ Journal of Agriculture,21)

other side¶

1 n. the other side: (favouring prepositions FROM, OF, OVER) the other side of the Tasman, Australia [AND 1. part of Australia remote from the speaker 1827 2a. a place in the northern hemisphere, esp. England 1892 b. in W.A., eastern states]

SKEY On 'the other side', Sam, you'd have swung on a tree, / To warn other liars about; / But, respecting your age, we'll let you go free. / With just a mild taste of the knout. [ New Zealanders signify by this expression, Australia.] (1899,Pirate Chief and the Mummy's Complaint..., The,Page 129)

SKEY On 'the other side', Sam, you'd have swung on a tree, / To warn other liars about; / But, respecting your age, we'll let you go free. / With just a mild taste of the knout. [ New Zealanders signify by this expression, Australia.] (1899,Pirate Chief and the Mummy's Complaint..., The,129)

He made the mistake [of] ... describing himself as an Australian ! Some ... have peculiar ideas about our neighbours `on the other side'. (Apr 7, 1906,NZ Truth,42/5/8)

BOOTH [In the accommodation house were] the villainous-looking 'lag' from 't'otherside', the bullock puncher, whose every alternate word was a profane oath, the stockrider, in his guernsey shirt and knee boots ... (1912,Five Years in NZ,39)

ON THE OTHER SIDE. .. The first impression that a New Zealander gets in travelling through Australia is that of difference. (Apr 6, 1893,Otago Witness,49)

2 n. the other side: (from a northern hemisphere point of view) the Antipodes, the other side of the world

New Zealand is thus emphatically "on the other side" the Ball." [sic] It is the nearest land to England's antipodes, and in our voyage thither it is almost a matter of indifference whether we turn to the right or the left, - the east or the west, for it is within a few miles of where the longitudes, east and west, coincide. (Sep 6, 1839,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,7)

Such remissness as this is highly blameable, especially when it is an English mail, as persons are always anxiously waiting for news from their friends at the other side of the globe. (Feb 11, 1843,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,2)

3 n. the other side: (Stewart Islanders') =BLUFF (cf OVER THERE

Stewart Islanders refer to the rest of New Zealand as "the other side" and, perhaps for that reason, they have managed to retain a community and a set of manners which enable people to live in peace. (Feb 3, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),7)

DEMPSEY And we are apt to say, when speaking of a person who is away, that he is `off the Island'. If we are told that someone has gone over to the Other Side ... we understand he has gone to Invercargill or to Bluff. Where the North Islander talks of his beach cottage, or bach, we call these little places cribs, a much more euphonious name. Ibid. 92 It was sad to have to disillusion these nice people and confess that we rarely saw an oyster, unless we crossed over to the Other Side and saw them in the fish shops of Invercargill. The far-famed Stewart Island oysters are dredged from Foveaux Strait ... (1964,Little World of Stewart island,6)

NEWTON The lambs which are sold out of the wool, in August, go direct to Nelson butchers. (1973,Big Country of the South Island,38)

Ewes out of the wool slipped back from the previous year's high values. (Nov 8, 1951,NZ Farmer,42)

NEWTON These [wether lambs] are sold out of the wool the following spring. (1972,In the Wake of the Axe,166)

Another argument in favour of at least twice yearly shearing is young sheep do much better 'out of the wool.' (May 2002,Growing Today,58)

out on its own like a country shithouse¶

phr. unique

McGILL out on its own, like a country shithouse excellent or unique; eg `Television has introduced somestartling logos over the years, but this one is out on its own, like a country shithouse.' c. 1910. (1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,80)

out on its pat¶

phr. out on its own; very good [c. 1914 (C.R. Carr).]

out on one's own¶

phr. beyond reproach

PARTRIDGE Now don't say that about my tin plate or I'll go crook: He is out on his own and, in fact, he is shook on me. Ibid. 288 You'll do me, D! You are out on your own. (1935,Slang Today,287) [NOTES: 1935 A.E. Strong WW1 scene in Partridge Slang Today]

out the back¶

1 phr. (Rural) the farthest reaches of a property, near to the back boundary.

.. the rolling contour adds interest while out the back, flat paddocks make tending livestock an easy proposition. (Nov 17, 2001,Manawatu Evening Standard,14)

PORTER It was then I headed 'out the back' and became a hunter for deer hides. (1970,Under the Nor'West Arch,47)

We trust you will promptly deny anyallegations that our disappearance fromyour esteemed journal is due to the factthat we are (a) out the monk (b) absentwithout leave (c) in the boob, and (d) onthe snorer. (May 21, 1945,NZEF Times,IV/204 : 5)

To facilitate the working of the station, there were three places where the hands resided. The Homestead, ... the chief one, was at the end of the station nearest civilisation. About eight miles further back was a cottage, known as the `Out-Station' ... [Further back] the Ruahine whare ... This whare was therefore the furthest back habitation ... Out-station . - A separate establishment where men live and there is usually an overseer, and where part of the station work is carried out. There were never many o.ss. in Canterbury, though The Levels had two and several other stations one. In Australia (whence, of course, the word came) they seem to call any hut with yards an os. ([1898 Morris Austral-English], Butler First Year ], p. 132) (Nov 11, 1933,Press, The,15) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

NEWTON (gloss) Some high country stations are so extensive that it is necessary to have a second homestead situated at some distant part of the property. Such a place is termed an `out-station'. On Molesworth station they even had a sub-manager at the out-station (Tarndale) in the early days. (1947,Wayleggo)

BEATTIE The custom in the old days was to call the buildings on a run the station,' and the land that went with it wasthe run.' On a big property you could find several stations,' and the principal one was calledthe home station,' while the others were out-stations.' This was the old Australianusage which was carried over to New Zealand at first, but gradually the signification was altered by loosely calling the whole run a station, and then the real station became a homestead and the out-stations became shepherds' huts. In this present book it will be noticed that generally thewriter has preserved the olden custom of calling the main buildings the station and the land the run. The runs over in Australia being, as a rule, much larger than those in New Zealand, thestation' and out-station' phraseology was preserved much longer than over here, where even inadvertisements in the late 'fifties we see the English wordhomestead' ousting the colonial word `station.' (1947,Early Runholding in Otago,11)

McLEOD In that case, there will be a main station and one or two out- stations with huts for musterers and perhaps even a permanent cook or married man. (1951,High Country,11)

STRONACH Having no horse, I decided to go by car to Jollie's Pass and walk with my swag to Tarndale, Molesworth's out-station, about forty miles from Hanmer. (1953,Musterer on Molesworth,2)

outcrop¶

n.

McARA Outcrop: The exposed portion of a reef, where it projects above the ground. The Martha Reef was the only one in the Martha Hill area which outcropped, the Royal coming in at No 4 level and the Edward at about No 5 level. OED In Mining and Geol. The cropping out or emergence of a stratum or vein at the surface; or the edge of the stratum or vein that thus crops out [cf the second use, in the quot. above] (1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),328)

The geological map was made by using as control points the creeks and streams, ... the position and extent of shallow underground mine workings, and their connections with the surface (locally known as `outcrops'), and surveyed outcrops of coal. MCMILLAN The man who takes the outside beats is weather burnt and trim. (2000,Country Bloke and Other Verse, The,249)

outside hole¶

n. [(goldminers')]

GORDON If gold was found in flat valleys the lead was always supposed to follow their course; whereas in many instances it went right across their course: and while experienced diggers were engaged in sinking prospecting holes and shafts up and down the valley, a new chum, who had never see agoldfield before, would often come and put down what was termed an `outside hole', and strike the lead as rich as ever. (1889,Miner's Guide, A,72)

overland¶

Valpy ... made the journey overland from Christchurch in twelve days' hard travelling, with a view to see the carrying capacity of the country and the feasibility of driving stock across it, and the rivers especially. (1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,150)

Arrangements might be made for sending a mail periodically overland to the Bay of Islands. (May 2, 1840,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,2)

Christchurch. 1st April. The Hon. the Premier arrived overland from Hokitika on Saturday. (Apr 1, 1872,Evening Post,2)

overrun a head¶

phr. (Rural) [sheepdogs] to make too long an arc when heading sheep.

HARTLEY Over-running the head. This is not as serious as short heading providing the dog is quickly manoeuvred into the correct position. (1949,Shepherd's Dogs, The,71)

Matheson's Snow showed great speed but was slightly wide at the top of his cast, and then he slightly overran his head. (Jul 5, 1951,NZ Farmer,32)

MILLS ET AL If Boy stops to the right of this line he is said to have 'short-headed.' On the other hand if he goes past the line he has 'overrun his head.' If the sheep is facing right and he continues on to the straight-line position he has also overrun his head. From a distance it is often difficult to be sure if a dog has stopped on the point of balance, or overrun his head or has short-headed. (1964,Practical Guide to Handling Dogs and Stock,36)

overseas experience¶

n. (See also OE)

REILLY They were an attractive couple at training college, and their `overseas experience' had made them even more so. They had come back from the years in Britain to learn the maori language ... (1986,Deputy Head,17)

OVENDEN O.E., as every New Zealander knows, means Overseas Experience. The term refers, as in such colloquial phrases as: He's gone to get his O.E., eh,'; orGot your O.E. yet then?' to that most rare of concepts, an abstract consumer durable. It has little in common with the Australian vernacular O.S.', which is a mere descriptive term, as inHe's gone O.S. and won't come back.' (1986,O.E.,6)

Your overseer has gone to Tarndale [station] and shot Charlie the German, and shot himself. (Feb 22, 1868,Marlborough Express,6)

FERGUSON But by-and-bye the overseer/ [168] Rode out to learn the shepherds'cheer./ (1883,Castle Gay,167)

BOOTH The station [c1859], or homestead, stood on a plateau some fifty feet above the plain; it consisted of two huts, mud-walled and thatched with snow grass. One of these contained the general kitchen ..., the other was the residence of the squatter and his overseer. (1912,Five Years in NZ,18)

Overseer. - Under manager. Os. were kept on most of the large stations in former days, and where the owner lived on the station he often managed it himself but kept an o. The word is rarely used here now. but Os. all call themselves managers; but the real difference between them is that a m. can sign cheques and an o. can't. The word was brought here from Australia by theShagroons. (C.R., p. 10;Butler A First Year in Canterbury Settlement] p. 70) (Nov 11, 1933,Press, The,15) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

overwind¶

n.

McARA Overwind: Failure by the engine-driver to stop his machine before the safety detaching-hook on the winding-rope came into contact with the detaching ring mounted in the headframe, thus suspending the cage in the skyshaft and allowing the rope to go over the head-sheave pulley. Overwinds had to be reported to the Inspector of Mines. To prevent overwind there were generally two warning bells which rang in the engine-room as the cage was approaching the surface. (1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),328)

Oxford scholar¶

n. five-shilling (piece or sum); dollar [rhyming slang]

PARTRIDGE Oxford scholar. Five shillings (piece or sum): New Zealanders' rhyming s. on dollar: C. 20. Also from ca. 1870, in the S.W. of England; now ob. Cf. shirt collar (Everyman, March 26, 1931.) (1937,Dictionary of Slang,596)

Oxford Scholar' is occasionally heard for five shillings; it probably comes from English rhyming slang fordollar'. (1943,English as it is spoken in NZ,BENNETT,91 ed.)

NZ rhyming slang: Rhodes Scholar on dollar. Also from ca 1870 in SW of England. (1949,Dictionary of Slang,20c)

HARGREAVES Crown [:] dollar, bull, `oxford scholar' (1972,From Beads to Banknotes,151)

The crown was called a bull, a cartwheel, a caser, five bob, and most familiar of all a dollar, the latter dating from the time when the rate of exchange was four US dollars to the pound. The only instance recorded of Cockney rhyming slang term for a coin is Oxford scholar, the derivation from dollar being obvious. The half crown was called a half-dollar, a half-bull, half-caser, two-and-a-kick, and two-and-a-buck. In the latter instance buck stands for sixpence, though apparently this term was rarely used by itself for that coin denomination, but almost always in association with a number of shillings. The florin seems to have had fewer English nicknames, only two bob being recorded as being in use in New Zealand. (Oct 1977,NZ Numismatic Journal,15)

oxidised country¶

n.

McARA Oxidised country: This referred to country rock in which the iron minerals had been oxidised byexposure to the air above the permanent water-level giving it a brown instead of a bluish colour. The brown could be due to the oxidation of pyrite to limonite and haematite in the rock. Near the water-level there was sometimes a zone of semi-sulphides where the ore might be enriched by leaching downward of some of the values. (1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),328)

n.

oxygen weed [=] Canadian pondweed: egeria: lagarosiphon [s] (1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,55)

oyster¶

1 n. . [see also Morris entry 1898 See AUCKLAND ROCK, BLUFF, DREDGE, FOVEAUX STRAIT, PACIFIC, STEWART, MUD]

ACCLIMATISING OYSTERS. (May 4, 1917,Grey River Argus,3)

SHEPHERD He shot several ducks Red bills a wood hen and killed a hare seal. ... cockles Mussels & oisters are plentiful and excellent. (1826,Journal) [NOTES: 1826 Shepherd Journal in 1940 Howard Rakiura 357]

McDONNELL The rivers are well stocked with fine fish ... - the very creeks swarm with them. There are abundance of lobsters, crawfish, oysters, prawns, and shrimps; besides clams, peppies, muscles, limpets, and cockles. (1834,Extracts From a Journal,15,1979 ed.)

YATE Those saltwater fish] most plentiful, and of greatest note, are, soles, mackarel, cod-fish, a species of salmon, whiting, snapper, mullet, bream, skate, gurnards, and a few smaller kinds, some not near so large as a sprat; with an abundance of crayfish, oysters, shrimps, prawns, muscles, and cockles. (1835,New Zealand (various authors/titles),71)

POLACK fn Among the piscivorous tribes, are those well known in Europe under the appellations of snapper, tench, haddocks, pollock, salmon, gurnets, john dory, hakes, bream, sword-fish, elephant-fish, diodons, pipe-fish, cod, parrot-fish, flounders, skate, cat and dog-fish, sting-rays,eels. - Among the larger tribes that sport in shoals in the vicinity of the shore, are, sperm-whales, called para[as-acute] paraua[a3-acute] (physeter macrocephalus); black whale, mungu nui (black physeter); right whale, tohora (balaena mysticetus); finback, (balaena physalis); sun-fish (diodon mola); large-lipped whale (balaena mysticetus); large-lipped whale (balaena musculus); hump-back (balaena gibbosa); shark, mango (squalus); hammer-head (zygaena); pilots, (scomber); flying-fish (exocaetus volitans); and an infinity of others, besides a great variety of testaceous and crustaceous fishes that almost cover the rocks under water, such as clams, muscles, oysters, crays, cuttles, limpets, wilks, gigantic muscles, a foot in length, cockles, sea-ears, sea-eggs, star-fish, mutton-fish, &c.; from the latter the pearl is procured which they insert in the eyes of their carved images and boxes. (1840,Manners & Customs of the NZers,1/202)

POWELL AUCKLAND ROCK OYSTER (Saxostrea glomerata). Found only in the upper tidal rocky zone of the northern portion of the North Island and at the Chatham Islands. (1947,Native Animals of NZ,19)

It must be confessed, that our Auckland "Opposition" is fearfully heavy-handed .. It opens oyster with daggers, and goes to market to buy an axe to break an egg. (Feb 26, 1848,New Zealander, The,2)

A number of oystermen were fined for breaches of the Fisheries Act, by taking oysters in the close season. (Apr 3, 1886,West Coast Times,4)

Bottles of Oysters. Those wishing a tasty oyster supper at home this evening can buy a bottle of the freshest at Yarrow's Taupo Quay. (Mar 31, 1906,Wanganui Herald,7)

2 n.

This is below high watermark, and leased under the Oyster Fisheries Act, 1866. (Oct 7, 1874,Star, The,2)

The Auckland oyster is of the rock or deep shell variety, and, in the opinion of some connoisseurs, is better than the "Sydney rock". (Apr 30, 1910,NZ Truth,4)

Nobody knows exactly how the debate began; ... but probably every member of the N.Z.E.F. on Gallipoli took part in the discussion of the oyster question : Auckland v. Stewart Island. ... whether the delicacy and daintiness of the little rock were better or worse than the noble fatness of the Stewart. (May 25, 1918,Quick March,1)

The dredge oyster, Bluff oyster or Foveaux Strait oyster is by far the most popular in New Zealand. (May 1985,Metro,47/28)

PAUL Dredge oyster Tiostrea lutaria ... Alternatively Bluff, Foveaux Strait, or Stewart Island oyster. Native to New Zealand, with a similar species in Chile. (1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,166)

CARR Rock oysters are found around Auckland and northwards principally ... In the straits between the Bluff and Stewart Island large beds exist of the flat, or mud oyster. (1913,Country Work,25)

After an absence from the menu for four months, the popular Foveaux Strait oysters appeared in Bluff and Invercargill for evening meals and supper. (Feb 9, 1938,Auckland Weekly News,9)

The Act of 1866 provided that "The word 'oyster', whenever used in this Act shall mean the under-mentioned species or varieties of the oysters that is to say:- 1. Shore oysters, excepting rock oysters between high and low water-mark .. " (Nov 13, 1874,Daily Southern Cross,2)

The Picton Oyster Ostrea discordia [sic] is the next, coming South, of importance. Those gathered off the banks are very fine and large. Allthough they have not been found in any great quantities still I would not be surprised to hear of the discovery of large oyster beds off Queen Charlotte's Sound or in the vicinity of Cook's Strait. (Oct 13, 1892,Otago Witness,45)

oyster borer¶

n. [CC Ransom notes: Is this the DRILL or OYSTER DRILL (Urosalpinx sp) that bores holes in oysters: a marine snail? There are a number of snails that attack molluscs but U. cinerea is said to be the oyster-specialist.]

POWELL OYSTER BORER (Lepsiella scobina). This is less than an inch in height, but it plays havoc on local rock oyster beds. The animal uses its teeth (the radula) to drill holes through the oyster's shell and then extracts the oyster piecemeal. (1947,Native Animals of NZ,28)

WILSON Even then they [oysters] are subject to an ingenious predator, the OysterBorer which, as his name implies, bores a hole into the helpless oyster's shell - and devours it. (1981,Fisherman's Bible,172)

oyster bottle¶

n.

SANSOM A row of oyster bottles - like glass pickle-bottles - in the fish-shed, was the first bank', but depositors put their coins into these bottles not for themselves ... The bottles were labelledPennies', Church Mice' (that was the threepence),Bobs' (the shillings) and `Scotsman's Half-Crown (the florin). (1970,Stewart Islanders,125)

oystercatcher¶

n. Haemotopus spp. =SEA PIE, also called redbill, torea black or black and white wader of seashores and estuaries, having a long orange bill with which it picks out small molluscs and worms. spg also oyster(-)catcher

We have indeed only a vague idea of the number of volumes of Alison's history - from seeing them ranged on shelves, as countless almost as shags or oystercatchers crowded in a long row on a mud-bank when the tide is advancing upon them. (Jul 11, 1857,Nelson Examiner and New Zealand Chronicle,2)

[see seapie, black bird, woodhen for quotations] (1772,Cook's Journals,2/135)

FORSTER I shot two Curlews or rather Oister catchers, which are quite black & have very bright vermillion bills of the same structure as the English Sea Pie ... [ed. fn]Variable Oystercatcher. Ibid. 1773 May 13 2/276 ... abounding with New Zeeland black Shags, Oystercatchers (commonly called Curlews) ... (Mar 29, 1773,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/243)

NICHOLAS Towards the southward there are also water hens of a large species, rails in vast numbers, cormorants, oyster catchers, or sea-pies, penguins, and some other sorts of aquatic birds. (1817,New Zealand (various authors/titles),2/256)

POLACK The small noddies (sterna solida) abound in large flocks, as also the oyster-catchers and water-hens, whose excessive tameness has nearly annihilated them. (1838,New Zealand (Polack),1/307)

DIEFFENBACH After leaving the village we crossed to another bay, E-Taua. This bay also is spacious and pleasant. Kingfishers, oystercatchers, tuis [fn Anthochoera concinnata, Vig. and Horsfield.], andcormorants [fn Pelecanus pica, Forst.], enlivened the trees on its shores, and many birds were singing in the forest. (1843,Travels in NZ,1/59)

THOMSON Several kinds of birds are indigenous to the woods and waters; among them the oyster-catcher, bittern, kingfisher, cormorant, quail, wild-duck, mocking or [16] parson bird, parrots, parroquets, woodhen, pigeon, etc. (the [sic] gigantic Moa bird is believed to be now quite extinct). (1867,Twelve Years in Canterbury,15)

HAEMATOPUS LONGIROSTRIS, Vieil. Torea. Oyster-catcher, Red-bill. (1870,TrNZI,2/69) [NOTES: Potts Birds]

The following is a list of the birds shot ... Platycercus auriceps, yellow-top paroquet; Nestor meridionalis, brown parrot; Carpophaga Novae Zealandiae, wood pigeon; Haematopus unicolor, black oyster catcher, or red bill; Haematopus longirostris, pied oyster catcher; Ocydromus australis, Weka, or wood hen; Casarca variegata, Paradise duck; (1872,Proceedings Otago Provincial Council,39) [NOTES: Appendix. Black Stewart Island]

DOMETT `Slim oyster-catcher, avocet,/ And tripping beach-birds, seldom met/ Elsewhere.' (1872,Ranolf and Amohia,274)

HAY The Torea, or oyster-catcher (Haematopus longirostris) is one of the sea-coast birds, and is often to be seen about our tidal rivers. It is a black bird. (1882,Brighter Britain,2/225)

DOMETT Bluff oyster-catcher, avocet,/ And tripping beach-birds, seldom met/ Elsewhere - ... Ibid. 2/338 Oyster-catcher ..... G. Haematopus. (1883,Ranolf and Amohia,2/63)

MORRIS Oyster-catcher, n. common English bird-name. The Australasian species are - Pied, Haematopus longirostris, Vieill.; Black, H. unicolor, Wagler; and two other species H. picatus<./i>, Vigors, and H. australasianus, Gould, with no vernacular name. [1846] (1898,Austral English,333)

WILLIAMS Torea, n. 1. Haematopus longirostris, oyster-catcher, and H. unicolor, red-bill; shore birds. (1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,513)

GUTHRIE-SMITH ... a segregation of Oyster Catchers (Haemotopus ostralegus) five or six hundreds strong (1936,NZ Naturalist,51)

RICHARDS Haematopus ... longirostris ... The bill is shorter and stouter than the New Zealand type, ... Oyster Catchers are found in New Zealand, Australia and the Moluccas west of New Guinea. Oyster Catcher. Torea. (1952,Chatham Islands,79)

MORELAND Our boat was carried swiftly along a channel between silvery mud-flats, where red-footed oyster-catchers and long-legged stilts were running about. (1911,They Led the All Blacks,36)

Bird enthusiasts are in for a treat with a good chance of seeing .. shags, white-fronted terns, reef herons and oystercatchers ... (Nov 2006,Footnotes,26 / 8)

Birds - Indigenous. - Kiwi .. toria or oyster-catcher ... (Nov 16, 1861,Otago Witness,6)

The red-billed gull has earned a quaint reputation for itself on account of its systematic plunder of the oyster-catcher. (Aug 8, 1908,Otago Witness,17)

on the estuary the oyster-catcher considers / journeys (May 14, 1990,Listener (New Zealand Listener),114)

COLENSO A black and graceful species of Haemotopus with orange coloured bill and legs is common [near Tokomaru Bay - East Cape] ... The natives call them Torea.
